Big Worries for Big Oil --- As Crude Prices Slip, Profits May Follow; More Mergers Ahead?
The Economy
Big Worries for Big Oil --- As Crude Prices Slip, Profits May Follow; More Mergers Ahead?
By Chip Cummins
912 words
24 October 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
WITH CRUDE PRICES falling and oil-field costs on the rise, major oil companies have a big problem:
sustaining their phenomenal profit growth.
Exxon Mobil Corp., Royal Dutch Shell PLC and BP PLC have all relied on big oil-price jumps to fuel a streak
of handsome earnings gains and, in many cases, record quarterly profits in recent years. The three
companies reported combined earnings of about $25 billion in the second quarter; in the third quarter of last
year, they earned about $25.4 billion.
Many of the world's biggest publicly traded oil companies report earnings this week and are expected to post
strong results, thanks to high crude prices.
U.S.-benchmark oil hit a record of nearly $80 a barrel this summer. (In inflation-adjusted terms, oil was at its
most expensive in 1980.) Since July, though, crude prices have fallen about 25%, sapping the momentum
from short-term profit-growth potential at a time of rising costs and higher taxes. And if oil prices continue to
drop, that could drive new thinking among oil executives over whether to pursue big tie-ups.
BP, which reports earnings today, estimates West Texas crude, a benchmark oil type in the U.S., averaged
about $70 a barrel for the quarter. That is still higher than the average price a year ago of about $63. But
current prices are now hovering around $58. U.S. natural-gas prices and gross profit margins from making
refined products such as gasoline are also sharply lower than a year ago.
Even before the steep drop-off in prices, earnings had been blunted by side effects of the commodities boom.
Oil-field costs have skyrocketed for many projects because of higher demand for everything from steel to
software among energy companies eager to cash in on the boom. Competition for new prospects has heated
up, ratcheting up auction prices for fresh exploration acreage.
If oil prices stabilize or drop further, cost inflation could also subside. But costs generally take time to catch
up with swings in commodity prices. That poses a growing challenge to profitability in the short term.
Neil McMahon, a London-based oil analyst at Sanford C. Bernstein, says recent trends of lower commodity
prices and higher costs mean quarterly results this time around aren't likely to offer much positive surprises
for investors. In a note to clients Friday, he said he expects to see further evidence of cost pressure when
companies report this week.
A handful of big companies suffered significant production shortfalls in the July-to-September quarter
because of unplanned disruptions. BP partially shut down its big Alaskan oil field because of pipeline
corrosion there, crimping its output and that of its biggest partners in the field, Exxon and ConocoPhillips.
Shell continues to suffer partial shutdowns in Nigeria, amid violent insurgency attacks in that major
oil-exporting nation.
Oppenheimer &Co. analysts expect the major oil companies to post third-quarter earnings about 10% above
results from a year ago but about 10% lower than this year's second quarter because of production shortfalls,
cost and tax increases and moderating oil prices.
Many companies are expected to post higher profit margins from chemical operations and from marketing,
which includes sales at gasoline stations, partly compensating for lackluster commodity prices.
If prices continue falling, two other big questions for investors may emerge: Will the world's largest oil
companies curb their generous share-buyback programs? And will they start looking for acquisitions, now that
share prices have fallen along with commodity markets, making some targets look cheaper?

--- Page 181 ---
Page 181 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
So far this year, BP has showered investors with $12.3 billion in buybacks and dividends. In July, Exxon said
it would increase spending on buybacks to $7 billion in the third quarter, up from $6 billion in the second
quarter.
But shareholder payouts can be sensitive to commodity-price swings. For instance, BP has said it expects to
deliver about $65 billion to shareholders through 2008 if crude sticks at $60 a barrel and if U.S. natural-gas
prices and refining margins stay lofty. Those expected proceeds would fall more than 20% if commodity-price
assumptions were revised down to the $40-a-barrel level.
Jeroen van der Veer, Shell's chief executive, has said repeatedly that he didn't see value in making big
purchases amid recent high oil prices. Yesterday, he announced Shell had made an approach to the board of
Shell's main Canadian affiliate, with an offer to buy out minority shareholders for about C$7.7 billion, or about
$6.8 billion.
In an interview, Mr. van der Veer said the timing of the approach was more about creating long-term value,
not about getting a bargain at today's lower oil prices. He said the current oil price "is probably one
component of a total mix" of factors determining the decision to pursue the deal, and said Shell hadn't
changed its acquisition strategy.
Fanning speculation of a fresh round of oil-patch deals, Premier Oil PLC, a small British independent oil
company, said yesterday it had received a buyout proposal from an unnamed party. Premier, with a market
capitalization of about GBP 1 billion, or $1.9 billion, said negotiations were at "an extremely early stage."
---
Jeffrey Ball and Russell Gold contributed to this article.

Home Run: Dow's First Close at 12000 --- On Anniversary of 1987 Crash, Funds, Big Stocks Help Nudge Index Past a Coy Milestone
Thursday's Markets
Home Run: Dow's First Close at 12000 --- On Anniversary of 1987 Crash, Funds, Big Stocks Help
Nudge Index Past a Coy Milestone
By Gregory Zuckerman
1,253 words
20 October 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
POWERED BY once-skeptical investors rediscovering a passion for stocks, the Dow Jones Industrial
Average yesterday closed above the 12000 mark for the first time, after testing the milestone for days. It looks
as if there are more potential converts still on the sidelines.
The newfound enthusiasm comes from an array of sources, including small-time investors pouring money into
mutual funds and hedge funds investing money for deep-pocketed institutions and individuals.
For all the excitement of the Dow's record close, which came on the 19th anniversary of the 1987
stock-market crash, the market barely nudged over the finishing line. The Dow, comprising 30 major
companies, advanced just 19.05 points, or 0.2%, to close at 12011.73.
"It was pretty balanced between winners and losers with the bulls just getting a slight advantage," said Jon
Najarian, chief executive of Najarian Capital in Chicago. "It wasn't like investors were throwing money at"
shares.
Even so, the Dow is now up a solid 12% this year, reflecting a recent embrace of big companies, especially
by hedge funds that traditionally prefer betting on smaller companies, where they can hunt for bargains others
might not have noticed.
The blue-chip companies' gains are part of a recent marketwide rally that has seen technology and small
stocks show signs of strength after months of dormancy. The Russell 2000 index of small companies is up
6.4% since Sept. 1, while the tech-stock-led Nasdaq Composite Index is up 6.7%, topping the Dow's 4.8%
gain. The Russell 2000 now is just 1.8% below its high, reached on May 5, and is up 14% for the year,
compared with 9.5% for the broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index.
The S&P 500 rose just one point to 1366.96, a 0.1% increase, and still is off 11% from its record high in
March 2000. The Nasdaq Composite was up 0.2%, or 3.79 points, to 2340.94, less than half its record high.
Apple Computer, a Nasdaq stock, rose $4.46 to $78.99, a nearly 6% jump.
The Dow was helped by a run-up in shares of cigarette maker Altria Group, a Dow component, which rose
$1.85 to $80.19.
Analysts say the climb could continue as long as interest rates and inflation remain low and more skeptics
abandon their doubts and start buying. There are also some dangers ahead: The market could be jolted by a
change in control of Congress following next month's midterm elections or by signs that the housing slump is
worsening.
Data suggest a sizable group of skeptics remains, which could give shares room to rise. Measures of bearish
bets in the market have remained high during the recent rally. Record amounts of stock are being sold short,
meaning investors are wagering that prices will fall by selling borrowed shares in hopes of replacing them
with cheaper ones bought later. In the New York Stock Exchange's latest monthly reporting period, ending
last Friday, so-called short interest -- a measure of how much stock is being shorted -- rose for the ninth
straight time. Through mid-September, the Nasdaq Stock Market saw short interest rise more than 25% this
year. (Related article on page B6.)
"You haven't really seen excitement among investors, retail brokers and institutional trading desks yet," says
Tobias Levkovich, Citigroup's chief U.S. equity strategist. "If investors get more aggressive, there is more
upside to the market."
One sign that investors are rapidly warming up to U.S. stocks: Last month, $2.2 billion was added to U.S.
stock-focused mutual funds, the second-highest monthly figure this year. And investors have added an

--- Page 185 ---
Page 185 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
estimated $2.6 billion so far this month, according to TrimTabs Investment Research, which tracks
mutual-fund flows. That is a sharp contrast to much of the past year, when investors chased international
stocks and shunned U.S. companies.
There also are indications that large investors are getting more comfortable with stocks. The Volatility Index,
or the VIX, a measure of the market's estimate of the stock market's future bumpiness that trades on the
Chicago Board Options Exchange, is near its lowest levels of the past decade. This suggests that investors
are "relatively complacent, and fairly optimistic," says Thomas McManus, chief investment strategist at Bank
of America's Banc of America Securities.
A number of investors seem to be pulling back from commodity investments, amid marked weakness in
energy prices and other commodities lately, and may look to redeploy their cash in the stock market.
Investors withdrew $214 million from mutual funds that invest in gold and precious metals last month,
according to Lipper Inc., a fund tracker. These funds added $129 million each month, on average, during the
past six months.
Hedge funds that focus on stocks have lagged behind the market this year, in part because they had doubts
that share prices would climb. Many are jumping on the bandwagon, especially in regard to larger stocks.
Large "speculative" investors, a category dominated by hedge funds, held more bullish than bearish positions
on the S&P 500 as of Oct. 10, according to Merrill Lynch. Two months ago, more of these investors were
betting against a rise in the S&P 500.
Global macro hedge funds, a category of funds managing $115 billion that bets on trends in various markets,
now hold positions that amount to wagers that stocks will rise further, according to Merrill Lynch. Just two
months ago, these big hedge funds were holding positions betting that stocks would fall.
Among the largest buyers of Microsoft, a Dow component, in the second quarter, were hedge funds Perry
Capital, Farralon Capital Management, Eminence Capital and D.E. Shaw, according to securities filings
tracked by FactSet Research. Hedge funds including D.E. Shaw, Renaissance Technologies and Citadel
Investment were buyers of Hewlett-Packard, also part of the Dow.
Hedge funds manage about $1.2 trillion compared with about $9.6 trillion at mutual funds, but their buying
could make a difference. According to some estimates, hedge funds are responsible for more than 40% of
daily stock trades.
Hedge funds hold a near-record number of short positions linked to the Russell 2000, which tracks smaller
stocks, suggesting they are becoming less enamored with smaller companies even as they warm to bigger
stocks.
Chris Johnson, of the research firm Schaeffer Investment Research, says he is encouraged that the high ratio
of options on individual stocks that pay off if shares falter to contracts that rise in value if a stock jumps is
roughly comparable to activity shortly before some past rallies.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks rose. Total trading volume for stocks listed on the NYSE was 2.65 billion shares, as 2,033 stocks
advanced and 1,268 fell.
Bond prices fell. The 10-year note declined 6/32 point, or $1.88 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the yield to
4.788%. The 30-year bond fell 11/32 to yield 4.911%.
The dollar weakened. Against the Japanese currency, the dollar fell to 118.16 yen, compared with 118.92.
The euro rose against the dollar to $1.2628, compared with $1.2534.

Peter A. McKay and Michael Hudson contributed to this article.

China's Reserves Near Milestone, Underscoring Its Financial Clout
China's Reserves Near Milestone, Underscoring Its Financial Clout
By Andrew Browne
1,502 words
17 October 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
BEIJING -- Sometime in the next few days, China's holdings of foreign currencies and securities will top $1
trillion -- a sum greater than the annual economic output of all but nine countries. The rapid growth in these
so-called foreign-exchange reserves has made Beijing a colossus in the financial world, cushioned against
shocks at home, but potentially able to trigger them abroad.
How China manages its growing pool of wealth has major repercussions for the global economy. Beijing's
reserves totaled $987.9 billion as of Sept. 30 and are growing by roughly $20 billion a month. That total
compares with the about $1.2 trillion in assets under management at U.S. mutual-fund giant Fidelity
Investments.
As the pot grows, the secretive and sophisticated portfolio managers at China's central bank are trying
gradually to boost their country's returns on its foreign-exchange holdings, at least in part by making
somewhat riskier but higher-yielding investments. Last spring, an unsuccessful effort to divine their intentions
sparked a steep run-up in the price of gold.
For the U.S., how China deploys its reserves is a question of some consequence. Most of China's currency
reserves are invested in U.S.-dollar-denominated debt, such as U.S. Treasurys, which are considered the
world's safest investment. That has kept demand for U.S. Treasury notes high -- and interest rates low. A
change in that pattern could affect how much Americans pay for mortgage loans and other borrowings.
Some in Washington and in world markets fear that China might one day dump its holdings of dollar-based
assets, setting off a tidal wave of sales that might swamp the U.S. economy. Despite such fears, there's no
sign that China is making a major move out of dollars and into euros or other foreign currencies, even though
Chinese economists have occasionally warned that the weak dollar holds down the value of China's holdings.
China discloses almost nothing about its reserves, beyond their awesome size. Roughly 70% of the Chinese
reserves are believed to be in U.S. dollar assets, 20% in euros and 10% in other currencies, including the
Japanese yen and Korean won, according to Brad Setser, an economist at Roubini Global Economics.
China's enormous nest egg adds to its ability to project its influence around the world. The prospect that the
Chinese central bank's State Administration of Foreign Exchange could spread its investments more widely
hangs over global markets, says Richard Yetsenga, Hong Kong-based currency strategist at HSBC. "People
think about diversification all the time," he says. The only other investors that rival SAFE in size, Mr. Yetsenga
says, are Russia and the oil-producing countries of the Middle East.
As China's reserves balloon, markets and many U.S. officials believe it to be buying less U.S. Treasury debt,
which is explicitly guaranteed by the U.S. government, and more debt issued by U.S. mortgage lenders
Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, which carries an implicit government guarantee.
China's shopping list also is said to include somewhat riskier but higher-yielding mortgage-backed securities
and U.S. corporate bonds. Financial-market traders and analysts also believe China has begun to dabble in
even riskier dollar-denominated emerging-market debt, operating through private money managers around
the world.
The country's massive foreign-exchange reserves are a direct result of China's effort to manage the
exchange rate of its currency. To prevent the value of the yuan from strengthening too rapidly and hurting
China's exports, the country's central bank buys dollars from foreign investors and China's own exporters,
issuing yuan in exchange.
The abundance of yuan created in this way holds down the Chinese currency's value. But the resulting
mountain of foreign-exchange holdings opens China to accusations from the U.S. and others that it
manipulates its exchange rate to make its products unfairly cheap on world markets.

--- Page 191 ---
Page 191 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Chinese central bankers also worry that the plentiful supply of yuan is overheating China's economy and
causing unwelcome inflation. Asked by reporters last month about the size of the reserves, central-bank
governor Zhou Xiaochuan said: "We think we've got enough."
China's reserves already far exceed what the country needs to protect its economy from global financial
shocks: $1 trillion is roughly 1 1/4 to 1 1/2 times China's total import bill last year and is equivalent to roughly
45% of the year's economic output. The total could pay off China's total short-term foreign debt six times
over. It dwarfs the world's single biggest mutual fund, American Funds' Growth Fund of America, with $147
billion in assets, and makes Harvard University's $30 billion endowment look like pocket change.
The latest data from the U.S. Treasury Department show that, as of June 2005, China's public and private
sectors held a total of at least $527.3 billion in U.S securities, including about $450 billion of long-term U.S.
Treasury or agency debt. The composition of its dollar assets suggests that China is a more sophisticated
portfolio manager than Japan, which is believed to keep a larger fraction of its $881 billion in reserves in
short-term U.S. securities. But the Treasury's figures may underestimate China's holdings because the U.S.
government can't always identify the ultimate owner of its debt.
Indeed, the Chinese central bank's State Administration of Foreign Exchange leaves few tracks as it buys
and sells assets through dealers all over the world, as well as through the largely state-owned Bank of
Communications in Shanghai. The sums are so large that traders and analysts from Hong Kong to London
prize even the slimmest details.
The flimsiest reports from China on the management of its reserves can cause gyrations in global markets. In
May, for instance, China Gold News, a leading industry publication in China, carried comments from a
Chinese economist who said China should raise its holding of gold to 2,500 metric tons from 600 tons.
The economist, Liu Shanen, doesn't speak for SAFE: he is a part-time economist at the Beijing Gold
Economy Development Research Center, a government organization closely affiliated with state-owned
mining groups. Nevertheless, the report helped push gold prices to a 25-year high.
By spending a large chunk of its national savings to buy U.S. debt, China is helping keep mortgage rates in
the U.S. low at a time when the money could be put to use in its own huge, developing economy. The
Chinese health system is collapsing; schools are starved of funds; social welfare systems are in dire need of
cash.
In the brewing debate about how the reserves could be better spent, Premier Wen Jiabao has suggested that
some of the funds could be set aside to buy high technology to transform Chinese businesses. Vice President
Zeng Qinghong advocates using the money to buy raw materials that China lacks. Meanwhile, former U.S.
Treasury Secretary Lawrence Summers has been barnstorming the globe telling countries like China they are
squandering resources by parking billions in low-yielding U.S. debt securities.
Some Chinese economists advise caution. Using foreign-exchange reserves to invest in oil and other
commodities is "far too risky," warns Yi Xianrong, a researcher at the Chinese Academy of Social Sciences, a
leading think tank. He advises buying fewer dollars and larger quantities of other currencies.
Just this month, comments by an adviser to the central bank that it was risky for China to hold so many
dollars sent the dollar slipping from multimonth highs against major currencies. But Fan Gang, a member of
the central bank's monetary policy committee, speaking at a financial forum, was quoted by Reuters news
service as saying that China had few alternatives to dollar investments.
U.S. officials currently worry less about China dumping its dollars than they do about the prospect that some
developing country, unhappy with conditions on International Monetary Fund loans, might end up borrowing
instead from China, potentially increasing China's influence in emerging markets.
According to officials involved in the discussions, the U.S. recently balked at letting China join the
Inter-American Development Bank as a "nonregional member," something China wanted to do to raise its
profile in resource-rich Latin America. Washington insists that countries such as China that borrow from the
World Bank aren't eligible for such status.
Demonstrating the enormous flexibility its vast reserves provide, China offered a compromise: For every
dollar China borrowed from the World Bank, it would put an equivalent amount from its reserves into the
World Bank through another doorway.
The deal didn't materialize. A spokesman for the Inter-American Development Bank says he can neither
confirm nor deny that such negotiations took place.

Banks Push Harder to Get You to Switch --- Services Aim to Ease Hassle Of Moving Your Accounts; Rerouting Direct Deposits
Banks Push Harder to Get You to Switch --- Services Aim to Ease Hassle Of Moving Your Accounts;
Rerouting Direct Deposits
By Jane J. Kim
1,271 words
12 October 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
D1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AFTER 53 YEARS, Don Neville has a new bank.
The retired consumer-relations manager stayed with KeyCorp's KeyBank for decades because it was too
much trouble to switch banks. His monthly utility, telephone and insurance bills were automatically paid from
his account, and his Social Security and pension checks were directly deposited there. "To change all that
would be a pain in the neck and time-consuming," says the 76-year-old resident of Ballston Spa, N.Y.
Then along came Citizens Bank -- a unit of Citizens Financial Group Inc., owned by Royal Bank of Scotland
Group PLC -- brandishing a higher rate, more convenient branch locations and an offer to handle all the
paperwork for him.
As direct deposit and automatic payments have made switching banks harder in recent years, banks are
increasingly dangling hand-holding services to get clients like Mr. Neville to defect.
Firms such as Citizens Bank, National City Corp. and Commerce Bancorp Inc. are offering to contact
companies and employers on customers' behalf to help change their direct deposits, automatic payments and
online bill payments from their old account to their new account. Earlier this year, J.P. Morgan Chase &Co.
began having its staff walk customers through the switching process in its branches. Previously, customers
had to download forms online.
These moves come as the percentage of customers who leave their bank every year -- a figure which has
typically averaged about 15% annually over the past five years -- has started to decline. The reason: More
customers are paying their bills from their bank's Web site, says Dan Schatt, a senior analyst at research and
consulting firm Celent -- and are reluctant to make a switch.
The banks themselves may be partly to blame for this. As they aggressively compete for deposits, they have
offered free online bill pay and rewards for signing up for direct deposit to get customers to stick around. That
has made it harder to get competitors' customers to switch accounts, which, in turn, is leading banks to
introduce new switching procedures.
The new programs come on top of banks' traditional enticements, such as free gifts and promotional rates,
that banks have used as a way to lure customers. PNC Financial Services Group Inc. recently began offering
to waive the first overdraft or insufficient-funds fee for customers who are switching. BankAtlantic Bancorp
Inc. -- which is currently passing out gift cards and George Foreman grills to new customers -- is offering to
pay customers up to $25 for their old checks and to shred those checks in its branches.
"We find that if a customer doesn't switch everything over in the first 60 to 90 days [after they open a new
checking account], they will go back to the old bank," says Deborah Sutor, vice president at Huntington
National Bank, a unit of Huntington Bancshares Inc., whose bankers also sit down with customers to fill out
the forms together.
Formerly, banks attempted to address this challenge with "switch kits" -- typically, simple checklists intended
to walk people through moving a checking account. Yet many prospective customers failed to fill out all the
necessary forms, leading banks to offer to do it for them.
Many banks are making a competitive sport out of grabbing rivals' customers. Fidelity Southern Corp.'s
Fidelity Bank, for example, pays new customers up to $20 for their old checks and keeps clear, giant trash
bags of shredded checks in each of its branches from people who have switched banks. The Atlanta-based
bank also keeps "scoreboards" in its branch lobbies with the names of customers' former banks.
So far, the banks' offers have attracted some interest from customers. Joe Shipe, a Monroe, N.C., store
manager, has five direct deposits and seven automatic payments that are tied to his checking account, which

--- Page 197 ---
Page 197 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
he recently moved from his old employer's credit union -- where he had banked for more than 30 years. His
new bank, Wachovia Corp., provided him with a switch kit outlining the steps that he needed to take to
transfer his accounts. With all the electronic transactions that he and his wife had set up over the years, says
the 56-year-old Mr. Shipe, "I wouldn't have known what to do."
Citigroup Inc.'s Citibank, meanwhile, will soon roll out an updated switch kit that will include more detailed
instructions on how to move accounts. The bank is also looking at services that will allow customers to switch
their account information automatically from another financial institution. Later this year, BB&T Corp. -- which
operates banks primarily in the Southeast -- plans to launch an enhanced version of its switch kit that will
make it easier to submit information online.
For the banks, there is a lot at stake. Customers who stay for more than a year tend to stick around for the
long term -- and their profitability almost doubles, says Richard Westelman, director at Dove Consulting, a
division of Hitachi Consulting, which helps banks set up so-called switch centers.
In the past, consumers might have had one checking account and one savings account at their neighborhood
bank, where they would often stay for years. Today, they are likely to have multiple accounts spanning
several institutions. And while the wave of bank mergers in the 1990s sent waves of disgruntled customers to
other banks, it also made it easier for some customers to stick with the same bank when they moved across
state lines.
Over the past five years, banks began competing intensely for checking-account deposits as they realized
how profitable those deposits could be, particularly if interest rates rose, says Michael Poulos, head of
financial consulting firm Mercer Oliver Wyman's North American retail-banking practice. "There is almost
nothing a bank will not do to get a consumer's checking account," he says.
Once the banks get the customers, they work hard to keep them. Today, consumers have more electronic
"hooks" than ever tied to their checking accounts -- which is why banks looking to poach need to get more
aggressive. Nearly half of U.S. households' recurring bills are paid electronically -- either through the bank's
Web site, directly at the billers' sites or by automatic services -- up from 25% in 2003, estimates Celent's Mr.
Schatt.
New technology promises to loosen some of that "stickiness" and make it easier for banks to deal with one of
the biggest barriers holding new customers back -- transferring online bill payments. Companies such as
Yodlee Inc. and Esaya Inc. are offering new products that allow banks to automatically move customers'
online-billing information from a competitor's bank.
More than 1,000 financial institutions have basic switch kits, according to the Online Banking Report. Some
services may be available only to certain customers. Citizens' Switch Support, for example, is available only
to customers buying its premium checking account, Circle Gold, which requires at least $20,000 in total
balances across the bank to avoid monthly fees.
---
Question of the Day: What is the most important consideration in choosing a bank? Visit WSJ.com/Question
to vote.
---
Trading Banks
Banks are making it easier for customers to switch accounts. Here's what
to look for:
-- New online 'switch kits' that will automatically transfer account
information from your old bank.
-- Ask for help from bank employees. Many will make the calls to switch
automatic payments for you.
-- Find out if the bank will waive any overdraft fees if transfers aren't
made in time.

Politics &Economics: World Watch
Politics &Economics: World Watch
682 words

The Wall Street Journal
J

English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
ASIA/PACIFIC
India's Banks Forced
To Lift Deposit Rates
As New Loans Soar
MUMBAI, India -- Strong demand for loans in India has forced banks to sharply increase interest rates on
deposits, but bankers say the gap between credit and deposit growth isn't likely to narrow any time soon.
As India's economy continues to grow strongly and newly affluent consumers borrow for a first home or car,
banks enjoyed a growth rate of 31% for new loans in the year ended Sept. 15, according to India's central
bank. Deposits in the same year grew 21%. Economists and bankers estimate that bank loans will grow at
least 25% to 28% in the year ending March 31.
With heavy demand for credit outpacing deposit growth, many banks are approaching the minimum
regulatory level -- called the statutory liquidity ratio -- which banks must keep on hand to cover their deposits.
The SLR, which includes government bonds, gold and cash reserves, is mandated at 25% of bank deposits.
During the past few weeks, many banks in India have launched advertising campaigns offering interest rates
at least a full percentage point higher than two months ago. Commercial lender State Bank of India Ltd. of
Mumbai is offering 6.75% on term deposits of one to three years, up from 6.25% in July.
The central bank wants to keep credit growth at 20% because it is worried about the impact on inflation and
credit quality if loans grow at a faster pace. It has raised interest rates three times since January, each time
by 0.25 percentage point. State Bank of India in August raised its main interest rate by 0.25 percentage point
to 11%.
"Even if the [central bank] raises rates again, it is unlikely to affect credit growth," said Samiran Chakraborty,
chief economist at Icici Bank.
-- Vipin V. Nair
---
AMERICAS
Venezuela Has Deficit
As Chavez's Spending
Outpaces Oil Gains
CARACAS, Venezuela -- Venezuelan President Hugo Chavez's spending has exceeded his government's
gains from oil sales this year, resulting in a deficit that many believe will grow in coming months. The Andean
country's accumulated deficit stood at 4.9 trillion bolivars ($2.28 billion) at the end of July, according to the
latest central-bank figures.
Venezuela's oil industry accounts for about one-third of gross domestic product and one-half of government
revenue. "We expect to see a deficit of $7.7 billion this year," or 4.3% of gross domestic product, said
Andreas Faust, an economist at Banco Mercantil in Caracas.
Mr. Chavez has continued to fund popular social programs that include free health care, education and
subsidized food, as he seeks another six-year term in office in December elections. He has raised salaries of

--- Page 203 ---
Page 203 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
state workers, continues to fund state enterprises and gives soft loans to favored industries. Many analysts
expect total spending to surpass 120 trillion bolivars or almost 40% of GDP by year's end.
State spending has pushed up prices for goods and services, resulting in 12.5% inflation as of September.
The Venezuelan economy grew 9.6% during the first half of the year. Central bank officials expect the year
to end with economic growth of 7% to 8%.
Most economists agree the Chavez administration has enough money to fend off fiscal trouble for at least 18
months in the unlikely case that prices for Venezuela's heavy crude basket fall significantly. Mr. Chavez has
set aside an estimated $47 billion in discretionary funds.
-- Raul Gallegos
BRIEFLY:
LA PAZ, Bolivia -- Bolivian Vice President Alvaro Garcia Linera said he is confident his government and
foreign energy companies can agree on new gas contracts before a Nov. 1 deadline. The "nationalization"
decree signed by President Evo Morales on May 1 called for the transfer of a 51% ownership stake in all
energy projects to a state-owned energy company, and said if new contracts aren't signed with six months,
the companies -- including Brazil's Petrobras, France's Total SA and Spanish-Argentine Repsol-YPF -- would
lose their concessions.

Politics &Economics: Japan's Leading Indicators Slip, But Outlook Remains Positive
Politics &Economics: Japan's Leading Indicators Slip, But Outlook Remains Positive
By Stanley White
396 words
7 October 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A4
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
TOKYO -- Japan's index of leading economic indicators stood below the boom-or-bust threshold of 50 for the
second straight month in August, the government said Friday, due to a narrowing in the spread between
short- and long-term interest rates and declines in Japanese stocks.
Analysts are optimistic that the leading indicators don't point to an economic slowdown, because stocks have
resumed their upward trend and the outlook for the services sector and the jobs market remains positive.
"The stock market is rebounding, which influences consumer sentiment and sales expectations," said Takuji
Aida, chief economist for Japan at Barclays Capital.
"Services demand is expanding, and that's creating labor demand, so going forward per-capita wages could
increase," he said.
Japan's index of leading economic indicators in August stood at 20, down from 27.3 in the previous month,
according to data from the Cabinet Office.
The leading index compares the performances of data known as good indicators for gauging the future
course of the economy. A reading above 50 points to economic expansion over the short term, while a level
below 50 heralds contraction.
In August, the coincident index, which measures the current state of the economy, stood at 77.8, up from 75
in the previous month.
The rise in August marks the fifth consecutive month that the coincident index has stood above 50. Gains in
industrial production, electricity usage and the ratio of job offers to job applicants contributed to the rise in the
coincident index, the data showed.
Separately, Japan's Finance Ministry Friday said the country's September foreign-exchange reserves rose
$2.53 billion from the previous month to a record of $881.27 billion.
The latest tally of Japan's reserves -- which include convertible foreign currencies, gold and International
Monetary Fund special drawing rights -- follows a $6.81 billion rise in August and a $7.06 billion gain in July.
Data released by the IMF showed that China retained its title as holder of the world's largest
foreign-exchange reserves. Beijing's foreign reserves including gold stood at $928.48 billion in May, while
Tokyo's reserves were second, at $849.29 billion. IMF data differ slightly from those issued by the Japanese
ministry because of differences in calculation methods.

Stock Mutual Funds Roar Back --- Large-Caps Lead the Rally And Investment Pros See Further Growth on Horizon
Fund Track
Stock Mutual Funds Roar Back --- Large-Caps Lead the Rally And Investment Pros See Further
Growth on Horizon
By Jonathan Burton
631 words
4 October 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C11
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stock mutual funds came back big in the third quarter and some money managers see the groundwork for
continued gains despite a softening economy.
After a dismal spring, investors in U.S. stock funds enjoyed a late-summer rally. Interest-rate and inflation
concerns diminished, allowing buyers to breathe easier and focus on continued strong corporate earnings
results. The bull market continued into the new quarter, with the Dow Jones Industrial Average reaching a
record yesterday. It rose 56.99, or 0.49%, to 11727.34, surpassing its former record close of 11722.98.
The Dow's gains reflect a resurgence of large-company stocks, which had lagged behind smaller and midsize
stocks over the last several years. Funds that follow the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index added 5.5% on
average during the most recent quarter, according to fund researcher Lipper Inc. Among actively managed
portfolios, large-cap value funds led the charge, up 5.4%, with large-cap core funds a close second, posting a
4.8% return.
Money managers see room for additional gains ahead. "Large caps look as cheap as they have in decades,"
said John Linehan, manager of the T. Rowe Price Value Fund, which invests mostly in large-capitalization
stocks.
Even beleaguered large-cap growth rebounded with a 2.6% average quarterly gain, and the category's
five-year record -- shredded in the technology-sector bust -- is again in the black.
U.S. large-caps -- which tend to do well in a slowing economy because of their earnings reliability -- could
carry fund shareholders for a while, particularly if economic growth proves worse than expected, some say.
"For a sustainable move up, which I think we will get in '07, two things have to happen," said Robert Doll,
chief investment officer of global equities at investment manager BlackRock Inc.. First, Mr. Doll said,
"earnings have to be marked to reality." As the economy downshifts, he noted, "It's hard to argue that
earnings are going to grow at double digits."
A second important catalyst, he said, would be that investors become persuaded the Fed will lower
short-term rates, a move he anticipates in the first half of next year. "Get those two things in gear," Mr. Doll
said, "and I think you'll have a better equity market."
When profit growth is scarce, earnings predictability and visibility matter more. Buyers will pay for shares of
companies that are considered likely to deliver. Accordingly, investors nowadays are shouldering a mixed bag
of caution and confidence about U.S. stocks, leading them to embrace funds that are staked to large
companies with dominant market share, deep cash reserves, a history of dividend growth, and experience
playing defense in difficult times.
Small-cap funds on average lost 1.1% in the period, Lipper said, while midcap portfolios eked out a 0.4%
gain. Weakness in small-cap growth stocks shaved 2.7% from related funds, and midcap growth funds fell
1.1% on average.
Mounting hopes that the Fed will engineer a soft economic landing, coupled with sliding energy prices,
brought down formerly highflying natural resources funds, which lost 8.4%, while gold-oriented funds fell
6.4%.
The quarter's most surprising result: another robust performance from real estate. The category added 8.4%
on average, better than any other sector group.

--- Page 208 ---
Page 208 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But observers urge caution on this corner of the market. "Investors adding commercial real estate to their
portfolios are on the right track, but they should be careful not to overdose on the asset class right now," fund
analyst John Coumarianos wrote in a recent report published on Morningstar Inc.'s Web site.

Page 209 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Dow Record: Thank Oil, Bonds --- Falling Energy Prices Reduce Pressure on Other Major Sectors; 'Robbing Peter to Pay Paul'
Commodities Report
Dow Record: Thank Oil, Bonds --- Falling Energy Prices Reduce Pressure on Other Major Sectors;
'Robbing Peter to Pay Paul'
By Ann Davis and Peter A. McKay
1,006 words
4 October 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
HELPING PROPEL the Dow Jones Industrial Average to a new high, oil prices continued their recent skid,
dropping 3.9% and boosting hopes among investors that lower energy costs will give the economy and
corporate profits a boost.
As the Dow romped ahead 56.99 to 11727.34, a record close for the first time since early 2000, its one
energy stock, Exxon Mobil, slid 1.59, or 2.4%, to $65.41. Of the Dow's other 29 components, 23 rose and six
fell, though not as much as Exxon Mobil.
Crude for November delivery fell $2.35 to $58.68 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange and is now
bracing 23.82% below its peak on July 14 of $77.03. Over that same period, natural gas -- which has been
falling all year due to swelling inventories -- has fallen 9.26%, to close at $5.759 per million British thermal
units yesterday. Gasoline futures have plunged 37.34%, settling at $1.4567 a gallon yesterday on Nymex.
The woes for the energy sector are good news for the rest of the markets. Falling prices reduce inflationary
pressures, meaning the Federal Reserve has one more reason to not raise rates. Moreover, energy-related
expenses fall, benefiting nonenergy sectors.
"Whatever comes out of the energy companies' profits helps everyone else in a very tangible way," says Mike
Thompson, research director at Thomson Financial. "You're really robbing Peter to pay Paul." Thomson is
forecasting relatively weak earnings at energy companies in the fourth quarter, up just 2% industrywide
compared with double-digit growth expected in the broader market.
The weakness in energy prices also spilled into other commodity sectors yesterday, reviving predictions that
the frothy commodity market has finally entered a slowdown. Gold fell $21.40 per troy ounce, or 3.6%, to
$576.30 yesterday on the Comex division of the Nymex, as the fall in crude prices reduced gold's appeal as
a hedge against inflation. Silver fell 59.50 cents, or 5.2%, to $10.95 an ounce. Copper, an industrial metal
used heavily in new-home construction, was down 14 cents to 3.29 yesterday and is down nearly 20% since
May as new-home sales slow.
But not all commodities are in the doldrums. And many economists say energy prices have fallen so far, so
fast, that it may be temporarily spreading gloom over other commodities markets that still have room to grow.
A recent forecast by Barclays Capital, a unit of Barclays PLC, placed U.S. gross-domestic-product growth for
2007 at 3%, Chinese growth at 9.6% and global growth at 4.7%, "which is significantly stronger than the view
that appears to be embedded in current oil prices," its analysts wrote last week.
Industrial metals with limited or falling global stockpiles such as nickel and lead are still hot commodities.
Nickel has risen 11.2% on the London Metal Exchange since the crude-oil peak on July 14, because
consumers are spending on stainless-steel appliances and factories around the world still need growing
quantities of stainless steel. Lead is up 21.68% over this period, in part because the Chinese are buying more
cars and car batteries. Although copper has dropped as new-home construction slows, it is in high demand in
electric-power transmission plants.
Some key agricultural commodities including corn and wheat also have held up as seasonal and weather
factors pushed them up recently. And gold remains up 11%, and silver 24%, year to date.
Kevin Norrish, commodities analyst with Barclays Capital, says that because the oil markets continue to be
well supplied, the market is ignoring the possibility that geopolitical concerns will return to push prices up. The
rapid drop "is encouraging selling by momentum traders. . . . We continue to see oil prices as having overshot
to the downside."

--- Page 210 ---
Page 210 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But the energy market is also coming to grips with a major change since 2005. After hurricanes Katrina and
Rita crimped Gulf of Mexico oil and gas production, and fueled lofty price expectations, the absence this year
of a big hurricane season reminded investors of how cyclical energy prices can be. Most traders have
dramatically scaled back their hopes for $80-plus-a-barrel crude any time soon.
At the start of this summer, "even though we were looking at . . . plenty of supply, the market kept on thinking
Katrina, Katrina, Katrina," says Stephen Schork, who tracks the energy and shipping industries in a daily
investment newsletter, the Schork Report. "By mid-August it was obvious this hurricane season was not going
to be above normal. Without that fear premium, prices were free to fall." Cooling international tensions with
Iran also played a role, he said.
Traditional stock-market investors have been gleeful at oil's fall. Some have even profited from the energy
pullback by simultaneously remaining bullish on equities and betting against commodity-linked investments.
William B. Smith, owner of a small New York firm that manages about $67 million for wealthy investors, says
that on July 3, he began betting on the decline of crude-oil prices by taking a short position on an
exchange-traded investment fund that tracks crude futures. In such a short bet, he borrowed shares of the
fund, expecting to buy them back at lower prices later.
Mr. Smith says he believes the stock market was -- and still is -- gripped by a "negativity bubble," despite the
new Dow record. He attributes the gloom to "all these super-spike [oil analysts] saying energy's going to put
us in a recession." To him, the "overly bearish attitude" about stocks "made no sense at all" and "all the hot,
fast money" going into energy was unjustifiably excited about oil prices.
(See related article: "Credit Markets: Interest Rates Drive Stock Market's Bulls by Boosting Valuations,
Investment Outlook" -- WSJ Oct. 4, 2006)

--- Page 211 ---
Page 211 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.

--- Page 212 ---
Page 212 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.

sales slow.
But not all commodities are in the doldrums. And many economists say energy prices have fallen so far, so
fast, that it may be temporarily spreading gloom over other commodities markets that still have room to grow.
A recent forecast by Barclays Capital, a unit of Barclays PLC, placed U.S. gross-domestic-product growth for
2007 at 3%, Chinese growth at 9.6% and global growth at 4.7%, "which is significantly stronger than the view
that appears to be embedded in current oil prices," its analysts wrote last week.
Industrial metals with limited or falling global stockpiles such as nickel and lead are still hot commodities.
Nickel has risen 11.2% on the London Metal Exchange since the crude-oil peak on July 14, because
consumers are spending on stainless-steel appliances and factories around the world still need growing
quantities of stainless steel. Lead is up 21.68% over this period, in part because the Chinese are buying more
cars and car batteries. Although copper has dropped as new-home construction slows, it is in high demand in
electric-power transmission plants.
Some key agricultural commodities including corn and wheat also have held up as seasonal and weather
factors pushed them up recently. And gold remains up 11%, and silver 24%, year to date.
Kevin Norrish, commodities analyst with Barclays Capital, says that because the oil markets continue to be
well supplied, the market is ignoring the possibility that geopolitical concerns will return to push prices up. The
rapid drop "is encouraging selling by momentum traders. . . . We continue to see oil prices as having overshot
to the downside."

--- Page 213 ---
Page 213 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But the energy market is also coming to grips with a major change since 2005. After hurricanes Katrina and
Rita crimped Gulf of Mexico oil and gas production, and fueled lofty price expectations, the absence this year
of a big hurricane season reminded investors of how cyclical energy prices can be. Most traders have
dramatically scaled back their hopes for $80-plus-a-barrel crude any time soon.
At the start of this summer, "even though we were looking at . . . plenty of supply, the market kept on thinking
Katrina, Katrina, Katrina," says Stephen Schork, who tracks the energy and shipping industries in a daily
investment newsletter, the Schork Report. "By mid-August it was obvious this hurricane season was not going
to be above normal. Without that fear premium, prices were free to fall." Cooling international tensions with
Iran also played a role, he said.
Traditional stock-market investors have been gleeful at oil's fall. Some have even profited from the energy
pullback by simultaneously remaining bullish on equities and betting against commodity-linked investments.
William B. Smith, owner of a small New York firm that manages about $67 million for high-net-worth
investors, says that on July 3, he began betting on the decline of crude-oil prices by taking a short position on
an exchange-traded investment fund that tracks crude futures. In such a short bet, he borrowed shares of the
fund, expecting to buy them back at lower prices later.
Mr. Smith says he believes the stock market was -- and still is -- gripped by a "negativity bubble," despite the
new Dow record. He attributes the gloom to "all these super-spike [oil analysts] saying energy's going to put
us in a recession." To him, the "overly bearish attitude" about stocks "made no sense at all" and "all the hot,
fast money" going into energy was unjustifiably excited about oil prices.

Investment Outlook" -- WSJ Oct. 4, 2006)

Over the Top: Dow Industrials Close at a Record --- Falling Oil Lifts the Mood, Which Is Far Tamer Than at High in 2000 --- Hope for...
Over the Top: Dow Industrials Close at a Record --- Falling Oil Lifts the Mood, Which Is Far Tamer
Than at High in 2000 --- Hope for Goldilocks Economy
By E.S. Browning
2,126 words
4 October 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
When the Dow Jones Industrial Average last closed at a record, on Jan. 14, 2000, it was a time of exultation.
A lot has changed since then.
After flirting with the record for days, the Dow Industrials finally topped it yesterday, pushed higher by the
hope that falling oil prices will support consumers and the housing market. The average rose 56.99 points to
squeak past the old record close of 11722.98 with a finish of 11727.34. Throughout the market, the mood was
more skeptical than during those heady days more than six years ago.
"We're in a vastly different world and a vastly different market," said Stephen Porpora, an independent broker
on the floor of the New York Stock Exchange. When the Dow registered its previous record, "we were at the
tail end of a bull market and everyone was somewhat drunk on the euphoria."
At the Big Board yesterday, as the Dow moved through its old high, jacket-clad traders continued shuffling
across the exchange floor checking prices and shouting their orders. When the market closed, they barely
reacted. There was no applause. Some traders said they were more worried about news that was trickling out
of layoffs at a couple of floor-trading firms.
There's good reason to be more cautious this time. Between the two records came one of the worst bear
markets in financial history. From its 2000 high to its 2002 low, the Dow fell 37.85%. The Nasdaq Composite
Index, containing many volatile technology stocks, plunged 77.93%. To push the Dow to a new high,
investors have had to overcome serious doubts about inflation and the sustainability of economic growth.
"A lot of people who were bearish are throwing in the towel and turning more positive," said David Briggs,
head of stock trading at Federated Investors, a Pittsburgh mutual-fund group. "A lot of people have been
sitting out this dance, and before we top out, I think we are going to get them in."
In that sense, today's skepticism could prove healthier for stocks than the excitement of January 2000. Stocks
are benefiting from a slow but steady flow of money from investors overcoming their fears, rather than from a
rush of irrationally exuberant buyers, as then-Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan suggested just
over three years before that bull market topped out.
The Dow almost finished at a record in May, only to succumb to the doubters. This time, it rode a surge that
began in mid-July and took the blue-chip average up 9.2% in less than three months.
The gains had a simple catalyst: the Federal Reserve. Beginning in July, investors began to believe that the
Fed, at its August meeting, might end its two-year campaign of interest-rate increases. Fed decision makers
did stop raising rates in August, and kept the "pause" going at their September meeting, although they left the
door open to increases later.
Hopes spread that the Fed's interest-rate management would foster a "Goldilocks" economy, with rates high
enough to blunt inflation but not so high as to torpedo corporate profits. Many investors see that kind of
balanced scenario as a formula for steady stock gains, and the optimism became so strong that the gains
continued even during September, historically the year's weakest month for stocks.
Now talk is spreading that the Fed might actually start cutting short-term interest rates sometime next year,
to give the economy a boost. The prospect of lower interest rates has been pushing down the yields on
Treasury bonds since late June, pulling many mortgage rates down with them. The prospect of
more-moderate economic growth has been pulling oil and gasoline prices down. Consumers are growing
more optimistic.
As a result, the stocks leading the latest rally have been those linked to consumer spending, such as
McDonald's, Microsoft, General Motors and Pfizer -- some of them rebounding from earlier slumps. Industrial

--- Page 217 ---
Page 217 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
stocks such as Caterpillar and Alcoa, which led until this year, have fallen back, as the pace of economic
growth appears to moderate.
The market looks much different from that of January 2000, a time when some believed stocks had entered a
new era in which "New Economy" stocks would rise for years. Technology stocks, which had taken on an
aura of invincibility, are viewed by many today as battered objects of suspicion.
Even fanciful market forecasts have taken a haircut. In 1999, two scholars at the American Enterprise
Institute startled Wall Street with a book named "Dow 36,000," a title that later drew ridicule. Shortly before
this latest record, one news release touted a little-known money manager's radical forecast: Dow 16000.
Skeptics worry that the new level reached yesterday might not hold for long. Their concern is that the bull
market is aging and that it could be too late now to pile into stocks.
The current upswing, which began in October 2002, has run for almost four years and is the fourth longest
since 1900, according to Ned Davis Research in Venice, Fla. But it hasn't been particularly strong, a reflection
of investor reservations following the excesses of the 1990s and the bust of 2000-02. The Dow's 61% gain
from the closing low of 7286.27 on Oct. 9, 2002, remains below the 85% average total gain for bull markets
since 1900. (Generally speaking, a bull market is a 20% rise from a bear-market low; a bear market is the
opposite.)
The more volatile Nasdaq composite index has doubled since its own 2002 low, and the Standard &Poor's
500-stock index is up 72%. But because the Nasdaq fell so far during the bear market, it would have to more
than double again to return to its March 2000 record of 5048.62. It finished yesterday at 2243.65.
The S&P 500, which also holds more tech stocks than does the Dow and also fell harder during the bear
market, finished at 1334.11. It would have to rise 14.5% to get back to its record of 1527.46, also reached in
March 2000.
The new record for the Dow Industrials means they have recaptured their losses, but it also means they have
made virtually no progress in more than six years. Of the 30 stocks in the Dow average, only 10 have
surpassed their levels of January 2000.
In general, the biggest losers since then are the big winners from the late 1990s. Microsoft, for all its gains
this past summer, remains down 46% since Jan. 14, 2000. Intel is off 60%. General Motors -- not one of the
'90s big winners -- has fallen 59%.
And taking inflation into account, the Dow still is 20% from its old record.
The biggest Dow gainers are Altria, maker of Marlboro cigarettes and Kraft cheese, up 214%;
equipment-maker Caterpillar, 151% higher; industrial conglomerate United Technologies, with a 103% gain;
and Boeing, up 86%.
In January 2000, the world's biggest stock as measured by stock-market value was Microsoft. Today, it is
Exxon Mobil, whose stock is up 56% since then.
Another sign of the bull market's age: The Nasdaq stocks, the rabbits of the current bull market, posted some
of the largest gains early on but now are slowing down. This year, despite the summer gains, the Nasdaq
composite is barely in positive territory, up 1.7%. The Dow industrials are up 9.4% year-to-date, and the S&P
500, 6.9%. Other big gainers during much of the current bull market, such as energy stocks, also have begun
to slip.
Some stock indexes that led the way for much of the past four years, such as those measuring small stocks
and transportation stocks, hit records earlier this year, but then fell so hard they still aren't back to record
territory. These are indexes of stocks that benefit from a strong economy. Their recent weakness reflects
continuing expectations that economic growth will slow.
Typically, toward the end of a bull market, larger, safer stocks start performing better than those of smaller,
less-established companies, and that is what has been happening this year.
Prudence and diversification are in again. "Having all your eggs in one basket seems like a great idea . . .
until the basket falls," said Tim Swanson, who manages money for Cleveland's National City Corp., in a
recent note to clients. "As others brag about the extraordinary returns that their concentrated bets are
delivering, just remember that it takes a lot of eye-popping returns to make up for a couple of weeks where
you lose two-thirds of your money." He pointed to the approximately $6 billion loss suffered by hedge fund
Amaranth Advisors in natural-gas trading.

--- Page 218 ---
Page 218 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
For all the doubts, the bull market has shown surprising staying power. It has blossomed in the shadow of
terrorism, Middle East conflict, high energy prices and growing U.S. political discord. The latest gains have
come despite jitters about the sagging housing market, an engine of economic growth for several years.
Some investors seem almost inured to the risk.
But the stock market's strength may be masking the fact that many investors today are demanding higher
rewards for risking money in a changed world. "The terror issue is hanging over the market," said Mr.
Porpora, the stock-exchange floor broker. "We're at war and we all feel vulnerable." One measure of that:
Investors today aren't willing to pay as much for a dollar of profits as they were in 2000.
Back then, the S&P 500 traded at more than 30 times its member companies' earnings for the previous 12
months -- more than double the historic price-earnings ratio of about 15. Since then, stocks haven't risen as
fast as corporate profits. So today, the S&P 500 trades at a less expensive 17 times earnings.
"When the Dow hit its peak in 2000, there was a fairly wide gap between the stock price of mega-cap
companies and the underlying value of the business," said Bruce Veaco, co-founder of Focused Investors
LLC, a money-management firm in Beverly Hills, Calif. Today, he said, stock prices seem better related to
profits. "We see a lot of opportunity in names like Wal-Mart, PepsiCo, American Express and Coca-Cola.
They're all holdings of ours," he said.
The pace of stock gains has slowed since the late 1990s, a frothy time when a year wasn't considered good
unless major indexes rose 20%. After surging 25% in 2003, the Dow was up 3% in 2004, and fell 0.6% last
year. Increased investor caution also may help explain the unexpected strength of the bond market, which
many investors view as safer than the stock market.
"The analysts have definitely got religion," and there are a lot fewer outrageous stock forecasts, added Mr.
Briggs of Federated Investors. "Analysts are more conservative, investors are more conservative, and I would
be willing to bet that chief executives are more conservative. The good news is that that has helped prevent
us from going back into a bear market. But the bad news is that it probably has served to limit the gains."
Mr. Briggs added that in his view, "you've got to give Bush and Greenspan credit for keeping us in the game."
But he noted that "a hurricane, a refinery fire or one event in the Middle East" could send oil-futures prices
upward again, creating problems for consumers and possibly having a negative ripple effect on stocks.
A lot of other things have changed since 2000. Then, stocks were practically the only game in town. Many
regarded investing in bonds, real estate and precious metals as a waste of time. Stocks like Cisco Systems
and Qualcomm drove the average tech-oriented mutual fund to a more than 100% gain in 1999, while shares
of small companies and non-tech, "Old Economy" companies languished. In recent years, for many people,
U.S. stocks have ceased to be the investment of choice. They have turned things like Florida condos, gold
coins, developing-country stocks and junk bonds. Worries have spread about bubbles in those areas.
And yet, similarities to the old days remain. When investors start feeling bullish, they often turn, almost
reflexively, to tech companies, which they expect will provide the fastest stock gains. Just that has been
happening since mid-July.
---
Aaron Lucchetti and Ian McDonald contributed to this article.

Stock Market Quarterly Review: Merger Activity Sets Stage for Record Year
Stock Market Quarterly Review: Merger Activity Sets Stage for Record Year
By Dennis K. Berman
1,538 words
2 October 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C11
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE BUSINESS OF mergers and acquisitions took a slight breather in the latest quarter but appears poised
to regain its record-setting momentum as 2006 draws to a close.
The summer months historically are a slow period for the lawyers, bankers and executives who put together
big deals. This year, July, August and September produced about $748 billion of transactions world-wide,
according to data collected by Thomson Financial.
By most any historical measure, that is an astounding figure, up 16% from $646 billion a year ago, but it is
light for this deal-heavy year. In each of the year's first two quarters, about $900 billion in deals were
announced.
Deal makers say there is little to suggest that the corporate mergers-and-acquisition market has begun to
retreat. They point to many conditions that should pave the way for a fresh torrent of announcements by year
end. With stock-market indexes rising, and the Dow Jones Industrial Average nearing a record, corporate
acquirers are likely to have the confidence to dive into more transactions, amid a market that is craving
private-equity deals.
Through the first nine months, there have been $2.5 trillion of deals announced, according to Thomson, near
the total for all of 2005, which was close to $2.8 trillion. That puts this year on track for breaking the $3 trillion
barrier by December, a number more than 50% greater than 2004, and seven times the volume of the very
slow 2003.
"We may not have hit the high point yet," said James R. Elliott III, co-head of global mergers and acquisitions
at J.P. Morgan Chase &Co. in New York, who noted historically low interest rates, the ease with which
companies can tap the capital markets to raise money to finance deals and increasing corporate earnings as
factors driving the market.
Each of those has been in place since early 2004, steadily increasing merger activity so that it rivals the boom
of the late 1990s. This latest nearly three-year span has produced transactions across nearly every sector,
from energy and consumer products to media and real estate, a trend that continued into this year's third
quarter.
The buying spree isn't limited by geography, with Europe producing deal volume that exceeded the U.S. For
the quarter, announced deals for European targets totaled $263 billion, compared with $252 billion of deals
for U.S. targets, according to the Thomson data. That represents a significant realignment for the overall
market, which historically has been dominated by the U.S.
That trend could continue in the next decade, as European governments accept some cross-border mergers
that would have been politically untenable in the past. Meanwhile, another distinct set of deals is being
hatched to counterbalance that trend: The formation of large, national champions to compete in this
Continentwide consolidation.
Two transactions in the latest quarter show these dynamics at work: In September, German truck maker MAN
AG launched an unsolicited tender offer, valued at the time at $12.1 billion, for rival Scania AB of Sweden.
Italy's second and third biggest banks by market value, Banca Intesa SpA and Sanpaolo IMI SpA, combined
in a deal valued at $37.5 billion, the biggest domestic European bank coupling in a decade.
"The question is how to create global competitors in Europe while also respecting each country's desire to
control primary industries, such as electricity, telecommunications or airlines," says Marc Granetz, co-head of
investment banking at Credit Suisse Group of Zurich.
In the U.S., merger-and-acquisition activity was again dominated by private equity, with two of the three
largest transactions of the quarter struck by buyout shops. The largest was the acquisition of hospital

--- Page 223 ---
Page 223 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
operator HCA Inc., of Nashville, Tenn., for $21.2 billion, by a consortium of investors including Bain Capital
Inc., Kohlberg Kravis Roberts &Co., Merrill Lynch Global Private Equity and HCA founder Thomas F. Frist.
Including HCA's $11.7 billion in debt, the transaction ranks as the biggest in history, trumping the
longstanding record held by the 1988 acquisition of RJR Nabisco by KKR.
Not long after the HCA deal was announced, another group of buyout shops announced an agreement to
purchase semiconductor maker Freescale Semiconductor Inc., of Austin, Texas, for about $17.7 billion, the
largest technology buyout in history.
There were signs the latest LBO record may not be around for long. Across Wall Street, buyout firms and
bankers have begun playing a parlor game: How big will the next deal be? Consensus seems to have settled
around $40 billion, though the chatter has been as high as $70 billion. At $40 billion, the potential targets
represent the mainstays of the corporate world -- the type of company that would have seemed impossible to
have been taken private years ago.
Nearly anything is possible, says Chris Varelas, global head of technology, media and telecommunications
banking at New York investment bank Citigroup Inc., which helped arrange the Freescale deal.
"The very capital-intensive cyclical businesses were always seen as the last group . . . that could not be
touched by" a leveraged buyout, Mr. Varelas says. "We've now seen deals in those industries . . . . Other than
human-capital-based businesses, it's hard to see any sector that cannot be taken private."
"It is amazing that today a $15 or $20 billion LBO is met by the market with a yawn," says Paul Parker, head
of U.S. mergers and acquisitions for New York investment bank Lehman Brothers Holdings Inc. "We are
approaching a land where size is no longer an obstacle."
Some on Wall Street don't believe these conditions can continue much longer. They are looking for signs that
the economics or investor sentiment could change and bring the LBO activity to a halt.
"In the past when the cycle has turned, it's generally been due to the credit markets closing," says William
Rifkin, global chairman of M&A at Merrill Lynch &Co. "It just takes one surprise. But it happens."
"At one point we're going to hit a speed bump," adds Jeffrey Raich, joint global head of M&A at UBS AG of
Zurich. "But there are a lot of good fundamentals to how the capital markets have developed" to help spread
the risk.
Among deal advisers, Citigroup appears close to challenging the long-running hegemony of New York rival
Goldman Sachs Group Inc. Through the past nine months, Citigroup ranks $42 billion in advised deals behind
Goldman Sachs, which has the No. 1 slot with $755 billion of announced transactions world-wide. J.P.
Morgan placed third with $640 billion in announced deals, followed by New York's Morgan Stanley and Merrill
Lynch.
Citigroup also took the first-place spot for the third quarter, followed by Morgan Stanley, Credit Suisse, J.P.
Morgan and Goldman.
Among legal advisers, the No. 1 firm ranked by dollar volume of transactions for the past nine months was
Sullivan &Cromwell LLP, of New York, followed by New York's Skadden, Arps, Slate, Meagher &Flom LLP;
Linklaters of London; Simpson Thacher &Bartlett LLP, of New York; and Clifford Chance LLP, of London.

Stock Market Quarterly Review: Volatile Energy Prices Spark Large Losses --- Oil, Natural Gas Fall Back As Production Threats Ease; Two...
Stock Market Quarterly Review: Volatile Energy Prices Spark Large Losses --- Oil, Natural Gas Fall
Back As Production Threats Ease; Two Hedge Funds Are Jolted
By Matt Chambers
1,078 words
2 October 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C9
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
ENERGY PRICES DOMINATED the commodities market in the third quarter, as rapid price increases were
followed by faster declines, leaving some investors with big losses.
Crude-oil futures prices hit an intraday record $78.40 a barrel in July, as Israel rained bombs on southern
Lebanon in response to the kidnapping of soldiers by the Islamic militant group Hezbollah. Although neither
country is a major oil producer, there was concern that Hezbollah supporter Iran could become involved and
cut crude-oil exports from the Persian Gulf. Forecasts of an active Atlantic hurricane season that could
threaten production in the Gulf of Mexico contributed to early price rises for crude oil and natural gas.
The early increases -- which were based on potential, rather than actual, supply losses -- deflated quickly as
tensions eased in the Middle East and not a single storm that entered the Gulf of Mexico had a material
impact on production. Oil finished the quarter down 15% at $62.91 a barrel, and unleaded gasoline settled at
$1.5492 a gallon, down 30% on the New York Mercantile Exchange.
Huge price swings in natural-gas futures, which ended the three months down 8% at $5.62 a million British
thermal units after hitting as high as $8.545 a million British thermal units in the summer, took a toll on the
energy-trading desks of hedge funds MotherRock LP and Amaranth Advisors LLC.
U.S. reliance on foreign oil was a major factor in oil traders' decisions. After a cease-fire between Israel and
Lebanon, prices were buoyed by Iran's threats to cut oil exports if the United Nations imposed sanctions for
Tehran's refusal to stop its uranium-enrichment program. Prices fell as U.N. Security Council permanent
members China, Russia and France made statements favoring dialogue instead of sanctions and it became
less likely that the U.S. would gain support for tough sanctions.
Further easing concerns about possible future shortages was Chevron Corp.'s successful test of its
five-mile-deep Jack field in the Gulf of Mexico. While production from the field is years away, it was the
deepest successful well test in the Gulf.
Analysts are split on whether oil's four-year run is over, two months after many predicted prices would hit $80
a barrel. Bears say a confluence of calm weather, easing Middle East tensions, healthy inventories and
forecasts of slowing U.S. economic expansion marks the end of a price rise during which prices had tripled
since the start of 2002. Bulls say tensions with Iran, the Persian Gulf's second-biggest oil exporter by volume
after Saudi Arabia, are simmering and point to similar pullbacks in recent years, before prices went on to
touch their highest levels.
There is little reason for oil prices to return to near $80 a barrel, says Citigroup Inc. analyst Tim Evans in New
York. "Crude prices . . . have stirred supply and damped demand growth to the extent it stands the 'supply
can't keep up with demand' mantra of the last three years on its head," Mr. Evans says. While prices may
record a seasonal increase going into winter, he says, oil is unlikely to go much higher than $70 a barrel
unless there is a serious deterioration in the stability of the Middle East.
Other analysts, such as Phil Flynn at Alaron Trading Corp. in Chicago, say it is too early to call an end to the
bull market. Mr. Flynn points to late 2004, when prices fell 28% to $40.25 from a then-record $55.67 a barrel
in less than two months, before heading higher.
Mr. Flynn forecasts Nymex crude futures will average $65 a barrel in the fourth quarter and $73 a barrel in
2007. A sudden escalation of conflict in the Middle East or a colder-than-expected winter could push prices
higher, he says, and crude's decline could help the U.S. economy and improve consumer confidence.
Natural gas reinforced its reputation as a volatile commodity, surging in July amid a heat wave and forcing the
closure of New York hedge fund MotherRock, which had bet prices would fall. It came back the other way in

--- Page 229 ---
Page 229 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
September, with a 10% drop Sept. 14, resulting in about a $6 billion loss at Amaranth, of Greenwich, Conn.,
which had bet that prices would rise.
Money has been flowing into energy markets from hedge funds, which aim to earn quick profits from betting
on price rises and falls. Money also is pouring in from mutual funds and pension funds, which generally bet
only on price increases. The Energy Hedge Fund Center, of New York, estimates more than 500 hedge funds
have $67.4 billion invested in energy, while the International Energy Agency said in a June report that index
mutual funds had $90 billion invested at the end of 2005.
In the industrial-metals sector, copper finished down 0.1% for the quarter at $3.459 a pound on the Comex.
Precious metals were mixed, with gold down 2.4% at $598.60 an ounce and silver rising 5.7% to $11.45 an
ounce.
Crude oil's climb in recent years helped gold prices, which rose partly on speculation that rising energy prices
would spur inflation. Many investors use gold as a hedge against inflation, because the precious metal is
seen to hold its value amid rising prices more than paper securities or cash. Gold also is used as a hedge
against conflict around the world.
"The price of crude has been one of the most talked-about parameters with respect to gold," Societe
Generale SA analyst Stephen Briggs said in the French bank's September quarterly review of commodities.
"This is largely because oil is seen as a proxy for geopolitical tensions, especially as the market has had to
contend with concerns over Iran's nuclear policy for much of the quarter, and also because energy prices
have been fueling inflationary fears."
Societe Generale sees the renewed weakness in the dollar helping gold, especially if the U.S. housing
slowdown intensifies. While a slowing U.S. economy bodes ill for oil, it is likely to have the opposite effect on
gold. The bank is expecting gold to at least test $600 an ounce in the fourth quarter.
