Hot Topic: The Channel Economy
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Hot Topic: The Channel Economy
863 words
30 September 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A8
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
If you're unsure about the direction of the U.S. economy, don't worry. Most of the "experts" are too, including
the gurus at the Federal Reserve. This is what happens at the stage of an expansion that a friend of ours
compares to a shoreline river channel: The tide is going one way but the wind is going another, creating a
significant amount of chop and turbulence.
The tide in this case is still coming in, with the economy continuing to show underlying strength. While growth
in the second quarter was revised downward this week to 2.6% from 2.9%, the main reason was less
inventory buildup. In other words, that growth reflected real demand. The third quarter that ends today has
also shown few signs of the great slowdown that many of our most quoted commentators have been
predicting for months, if not years.
The job market remains robust, with the unemployment rate at a low 4.7% and weekly jobless claims falling.
The big story in many parts of the country has been a labor shortage, especially for skilled workers and where
the crackdown on immigrants has reduced the labor supply. Employers are paying more to keep their best
employees, which is why wages have begun to rise more rapidly -- as they typically do at this stage of an
expansion. Combined with falling gasoline prices, this will help buoy consumer spending in the months
ahead.
Meanwhile, corporate profits and business investment keep growing at impressive rates. This has been the
case since the 2003 tax cuts became law, raising the after-tax return on capital investment. The GOP
Congress hasn't accomplished much this year, but one achievement was its extension of the 15% tax rate on
capital gains and dividends for another two years through 2010. (Credit Arizona's Jon Kyl, who almost
single-handedly pushed this through the Senate.) This maintains these incentives to invest past the 2008
election, when taxes will be a major battleground.
So what's creating today's choppy waters? The answer is that the economy is now adjusting to the Fed's
mistake of keeping monetary policy too easy for too long from 2003 into 2005. The Fed did an admirable job
of easing money in the wake of the dot-com collapse and 9/11. But spooked by what Ben Bernanke (then a
Fed governor, now chairman) called "deflation," and underestimating the power of the 2003 tax cuts, the Fed
let inflation creep back into investor expectations.
We are now living with the consequences of that mistake. A housing boom that in some markets became an
asset bubble has begun to come back to Earth -- painfully so for those who stretched the limits of creative
financing. Many Americans measure their net worth largely by their home values, so the housing slump also
makes them feel poorer. The good news is that prices rose so far so fast that they'd have to fall quite a ways
before they reach the declines last seen in the early 1990s. The slump will, however, take a bite out of GDP
for some time to come.
The other great adjustment is likely to come as commodity and natural resource prices adjust to tighter
monetary policy. Some of our friends keep saying that the rise of gold, oil, iron ore and other commodity
prices in recent years was merely a change in relative prices due to rising demand from China and
elsewhere. But we've never seen such a rapid price rise that wasn't also rooted in renewed inflationary
expectations. Gold only climbs above $700 an ounce from close to $400 in two years when the Fed is
creating too many dollars.
Some of these prices are now falling, as the Fed sticks more diligently to its antiinflation mandate. A decline
in oil prices will help the economy, but some investors who made big bets on natural resources will be
casualties. Just ask Amaranth Advisors, the hedge fund that may have to liquidate after one of its masters of
the universe lost $6 billion betting on energy prices. There will surely be other Amaranths, though a silver
lining is that its collapse has so far caused barely a ripple in the larger financial system. A few rich people
merely lost a lot of money; that's capitalism.

--- Page 231 ---
Page 231 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Today's choppy waters are teaching us once again how dangerous it is for the Fed to allow inflation to
infiltrate into investment decisions. Capital begins to move based on anticipated price changes, not on
underlying economic value or technological promise. The price and asset booms are great while they last, but
the workouts are painful.
A useful thought experiment is to imagine how the last four years would have gone without the tax cuts of
2003 to help overcome today's monetary headwinds. Growth has averaged nearly 4% a year, federal
revenues have increased by more than $500 billion over the last two fiscal years, and unemployment is low. If
tighter money leads to more trouble in the months ahead, let's hope Washington has the wit to cut taxes
again.

Page 232 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Dow Industrials Turn Away From Record High
Friday's Markets
Dow Industrials Turn Away From Record High
By Peter A. McKay
840 words
30 September 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
FRESH ECONOMIC data stoked inflation fears and hurt the prices of bonds and stocks, as the Dow Jones
Industrial Average fell further from its record.
For a second straight session, the blue-chip indicator briefly broke above its peak, reached in January 2000,
but could not finish there on the last day of trading in 2006's third quarter.
Unlike in Thursday's session, however, the Dow didn't just curb its gains on the way to a narrower rally. It
closed lower, down 39.38 points, or 0.3%, at 11679.07, up 1.5% on the week. Of the Dow's 30 components,
21 finished lower, with consumer-driven stocks like Home Depot, McDonald's and Wal-Mart Stores among
the biggest decliners in percentage terms.
The average now stands 43.91 points away from its record close. Despite missing the record, the Dow Jones
industrials gained 4.7% in the third quarter, its fifth-consecutive positive quarter and the strongest
third-quarter measure since 1995.
The Commerce Department reported that its index tracking the price of personal consumption expenditures,
excluding volatile food and energy purchases, grew by 2.5% in August, the largest increase in more than 11
years. The reading was also well above the 2% level that Federal Reserve policymakers mark as the top of
their "comfort zone," meaning that anything higher could indicate inflationary pressures.
Most Wall Street pros, especially bond traders, believe inflation is under control and the Fed will remain "on
pause" until it eventually has to cut rates, perhaps sometime next year. But that confidence showed some
cracks yesterday as Treasury prices edged lower, pushing yields higher.
Keith Hembre, chief economist at the mutual-fund firm First American Funds, noted that the growth in the
Commerce Department's personal-expenditures index, although eye-popping at first glance, was roughly in
line with July's 2.3% rise. "This suggests it's peaking, which is a good thing for stocks," Mr. Hembre said.
In the months ahead, he believes the Dow can eventually reach its record. "The way it's been grinding higher
lately, there's no reason to believe it can't," he said.
Other economic data also showed signs of strength. The University of Michigan said that its
consumer-confidence index rose to 85.4 this month, up from a reading of 82.0 in August. Also, the National
Association of Purchasing Management's Chicago arm said its index of local business activity rose to 62.1
this month, its highest level this year, up from a reading of 57.1 in August on a seasonally adjusted basis.
As has been the case recently, stock investors were ambivalent about such signs of strength, wary of
anything that might get the Fed back into tightening mode.
The S&P 500 fell 0.2%, or 3.30 points, to 1335.85, up 1.6% on the week. In the third quarter, both the S&P
500 and the Dow rallied about 5%.
The technology-focused Nasdaq Composite Index fell 0.5%, or 11.59 points Friday, to 2258.43, up 1.8% on
the week and up nearly 4% for the quarter.
Looking ahead to the fourth quarter, however, most investing pros are still optimistic, expecting solid growth
in corporate profits that should help buoy stock prices. "Our bias is that the markets most likely will continue
to climb a wall of worry," said portfolio manager Thomas Ross, of Allianz Global Investors.
Crude-oil prices were modestly higher, which also hurt the mood in the stock market. Oil futures for
November delivery edged up 15 cents, or 0.2%, to $62.91 a barrel, up 3.9% on the week at the New York
Mercantile Exchange.

--- Page 233 ---
Page 233 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Energy traders continued to wrangle over whether the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries is
about to cut crude production.
Gold prices also fell, hurt by strength in the dollar. The yellow metal usually moves in the opposite direction of
the greenback because gold in some countries is still treated as an alternative form of currency at times
when investors lose confidence in paper assets. Gold futures for October delivery fell $6.40, or 1.1%, to
$598.60 a troy ounce on Nymex's Comex division.
Outside the U.S., stocks fell in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. shares, fell
0.1%, or 0.2 points, to 219.95.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks fell. Total trading volume for stocks listed on the New York Stock Exchange was 2.2 billion shares, as
1,340 stocks advanced and 1,946 fell.
Bond prices fell. The 10-year note fell 5/32 point, pushing the yield up to 4.637%. The 30-year bond fell 4/32
to yield 4.767%.
The dollar strengthened. Against the yen, the dollar rose to 118.12 yen, compared with 117.77. The euro fell
to $1.2681, compared with $1.2706.

Currencies Falter As Commodities Lose Their Shine --- With Oil, Gas and Metals Down, Prospects for Canada, Chile Fade; Rebound Unlikely...
Abreast of the Market
Currencies Falter As Commodities Lose Their Shine --- With Oil, Gas and Metals Down, Prospects for
Canada, Chile Fade; Rebound Unlikely Anytime Soon
By Karen Richardson
952 words
25 September 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AS PRICES FOR OIL, gas and metals take a hit, they are giving the countries that produce these
commodities a run for their money.
The currencies of countries like Canada, Australia and Chile -- sometimes called "commodity currencies"
because they are so heavily supported by the export sales of natural resources -- are starting to fall as
commodities prices wobble off recent highs.
Since peaking in mid-May, the CRB index, a broad measure of the performance of commodities compiled by
the Commodity Research Bureau, has fallen about 15%. Oil is down 21% from its high on July 14, while
natural gas is down 59% in 2006. Gold, while up 14% for the year, is down about 18% from its 12-month
high. Copper is off about 14% since May.
All of this stems from hints of slowing growth in the U.S. economy, which translates to less demand for
energy and other commodities. Last week, the stock market closed lower as investors fretted over
softer-than-expected economic data. In an unexpected twist, hedge fund Amaranth Advisors reported big
losses after betting wrong on natural gas.
The broader market shrugged that off, but the Dow Jones Industrial Average -- which until recently was
approaching record levels -- still fell 52.67 points, or 0.5%, during the week to 11508.10, leaving it up 7.4% for
the year.
Investors have done well buying shares of natural-resources companies like miners and loggers for the past
five years. But with the commodity boom looking shaky, nervousness has risen. Also, concern about
speculators exiting these markets could cause more havoc.
Will commodity prices bounce back soon? A look at commodity currencies would indicate that such a surge
isn't likely anytime soon.
"The next five to six quarters are going to be tough," says Abhijit Chakrabortti, head of global investment
strategy at J.P. Morgan Chase &Co. "Where you could get a lot of concern is Canada and Australia."
Adding to the vulnerability of these main commodity currencies is how speculators such as hedge funds trade
in the foreign-exchange markets. Often, they use complex derivatives that can be difficult to sell in volatile
markets. When faced with selling pressure from either redemptions or margin calls, these investors are more
likely to dump their actual currency holdings than the derivatives, because it is easier.
"There's infinitely more liquidity in the currency markets," says Robert Kowit, head of global fixed-income
investments at Federated Investors Inc.
The Canadian and Australian dollars, up about 42% and 48%, respectively, since the end of 2001, have
edged off their highs in the past few months. The Canadian dollar, called the "loonie" because the dollar coin
features the common loon, has softened about 1.7% since hitting a high June 12 at just under 1.10 to the
U.S. dollar. The Australian dollar has fallen 2.6% to just under 76 U.S. cents from its high of about 78 cents
on May 11.
Booming energy prices have helped support the loonie, and Canada sells a great deal of natural gas and oil
to the U.S. Many analysts agree that Canada, at least in the short term, could be hit hard if the U.S. economy
slows significantly during the next 12 months. Canada relies on selling oil, lumber and cars across the border,
but its merchandise trade surplus shrank in July to its lowest level in more than three years.

--- Page 241 ---
Page 241 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The loonie's day in the sun may not be over, though. Analysts say it will strengthen again over the longer term
-- assuming the U.S. growth cycle starts again in about 18 months. That is because of Canada's wealth of
natural resources, especially oil and natural gas, which will continue to be in high demand for years to come.
"Canada has such a strong fundamental bid to its currency that it's hard to see the loonie falling very far,"
says Carl B. Weinberg, chief economist at research firm High Frequency Economics. "It doesn't mean people
can't get killed on a day-to-day basis, though."
John Rothfield, a currency strategist at Bank of America, estimates the Canadian dollar will end 2006 at the
current rate of about 1.12 to the dollar but will strengthen to about 1.09 to the dollar -- its 12-month high -- by
the end of 2007.
The Australian dollar has a similar outlook. Highly exposed to base metals and precious metals due to its big
mining industry, the Australian economy is poised to feel pressure if commodity prices fall further. The
Journal of Commerce's base-metal index has fallen about 4% since July and is expected to fall an additional
10% over the next several months, according to Sue Trinh, a currency strategist at RBC Capital Markets in
Sydney.
"That would limit the Aussie to go higher," Ms. Trinh says. She argues, however, that Australia's proximity to
fast-growing China makes its long-term promise greater than Canada's.
In Latin America, high copper prices helped to push the Chilean peso up about 22% against the dollar since
the end of 2001. Recently, economic growth has slowed as copper prices have fallen, prompting the Chilean
central bank to lower its forecast for 2006 gross domestic product.
The peso, which in December reached about 510 to the U.S. dollar, has weakened to about 540. Bank of
America expects it to soften to 570 in the first quarter of 2007 before ending at about 560 by the end of next
year.

Books: A Century of Slaughter --- Trying to comprehend the unprecedented carnage that accompanied extraordinary progress
PURSUITS
Books: A Century of Slaughter --- Trying to comprehend the unprecedented carnage that
accompanied extraordinary progress
By Norman Stone
1,351 words
23 September 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
P8
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The War of the World
By Niall Ferguson
Penguin, 808 pages, $35
THE 20TH century was a murderous one, far more so than any other. You can count the victims of the
Inquisition or the Terror of the French Revolution in not many thousands. But 20th-century war and upheaval
claimed millions -- most harvested over the course of a few decades. In "The War of the World," Niall
Ferguson ponders exactly what made the century "so bloody," particularly in the 50 years "from 1904 to
1953." There is of course no one answer. A thousand forces fed their malevolence and confusion into the
slaughter machine.
As for those dates: You might cavil with the first one -- it marks the beginning of the Russo-Japanese War --
and prefer instead 1911. It was in that year that Italy grabbed part of North Africa from the Ottomans and, by
doing so, stirred the Balkans into an anti-Ottoman unrest that started the countdown to general European
war. And then there is the odd fact that modern war began, in a sense, anachronistically, in the 19th century
with the American Civil War: For the first time, vast numbers of men died in a conflict fought with
machine-made weapons for a general principle.
But surely the exact dates are less important than the scale of the carnage Mr. Ferguson sets out to explain.
It is quite a record: two world wars, an aftermath in Korea (1950-53), and astonishing massacre and genocide
from Stalin and Mao. It was Stalin who said that a few deaths are a tragedy but a million are a statistic. Mr.
Ferguson's half-century contains, in that sense, many statistics.
WITH SUCH A THEME, Mr. Ferguson has set himself an enormous undertaking, but he is more than up to
the task. Again he shows himself to be a writer of extraordinary energy and versatility. In recent years he has
written a great deal on empire, a subject relevant to "The War of the World" because of the imperial ambition
behind so many of the century's conflicts, not least its world wars. He took up the first one in "The Pity of War"
(1998) -- a classic that argued provocatively against Britain's participation in the war itself (while parsing such
matters as the effect of bond prices on war finance). "The War of the World" revisits that ground but travels
through a much greater expanse of killing and political folly.
There is a sharply written history of World War II embedded in "The War of the World" and a dissection of the
social structures from which Europe's elites emerged. Mr. Ferguson can even tell you how the Saxe-Coburg
dynasty pioneered the notion of "bourgeois monarchy" (or, if you want to evoke the memory of Diana, a
"people's princessery"). He inevitably takes up the racial theorizing that drove much 20th-century ideology,
noting that 19th-century empire took for granted the miscegenation on far-flung shores. He cites a book about
old Haiti by one Moreau de Saint-Mery that listed 13 Creole terms for various racial mixtures. But by 1901, a
"world-wide revulsion" had set in, aided by pseudo-science and Social Darwinism. The racial ideology of the
Germans 30 years later, however extreme, was very much in the spirit of its time.
But Mr. Ferguson does not confine himself to the West. He has also done considerable homework on Japan
and appropriately so: Its trajectory from feudalism to industrialism roughly parallels Western Europe's. He
observes that the Axis alliance -- the 1940 Tripartite Pact -- was driven by a ruthless internal logic. Despite
differences in outlook, Mr. Ferguson writes, "Nazi Germany, Fascist Italy and Imperial Japan shared certain
fundamental assumptions about the character of the world they hoped to forge in the fires of war." It was to
be ruled "by three empire-states" that were themselves ordered by "centralized power." And it was to be a
world "shared between three master races: the Aryan, the Roman and the Yamato." Mr. Ferguson quotes a
Japanese diplomat saying in the early 1940s: "World totalitarianism will take the place of Anglo-Saxonism,

--- Page 243 ---
Page 243 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
which is bankrupt and will be wiped out." We hear versions of such apocalyptic rhetoric today, from the
Middle East instead of the Far.
Mr. Ferguson does of course recognize, as he must, that the century as a whole saw vaulting progress.
Hundreds of millions lived far better than ever before because of advances in technology and medicine.
When World War I started, great numbers of men died of disease or of wounds; when it ended, such deaths
were accounting for only 1% of the total butcher's bill. The French writer Louis- Ferdinand Celine referred to
the 20th century as a "vaccinated apocalypse": Mr. Ferguson is right to cite him as an emblem of interwar
defeatism shading into something more dark and, ultimately, sinister.
Celine -- wounded in World War I and invalided out of the army -- drew on his war experience for "Journey to
the End of Night" (1932), a novel that offers, in Mr. Ferguson's words, a "stomach-churning evocation" of
military slaughter. After the armistice, Celine traveled, saw French imperialism at work in Africa and then
toiled as a doctor in an unfashionable part of Paris.
Why are so many writers -- think only of Smollett and Chekhov -- doctors by training? Perhaps because they
see both sides of progress and develop a superb sense of black humor. That describes Celine's outlook, at
least in his jerry-built novels, quite untranslatable into English. At first he counted himself a man of the left.
But as the 1930s went ahead, he moved right and ended up in the Nazi occupation of France as an
anti-Semitic propagandist. A monster, yes. But if you are going to look into the history of the 20th century,
whom would you rather read: Celine or some worthier figure offering hope?
Mr. Ferguson touches on other literary eminences in the course of his narrative. He observes that Mikhail
Bulgakov (also by training a doctor), in the novel "The Master and Margarita," captured "the loathsome quality
of the [Bolshevik] Terror." He quotes from Evelyn Waugh's "Sword of Honour" trilogy, a superb study of
England in the depressing late stages of World War II, when all was rationing and form-filling and, not least,
the steady fading away of the aristocratic virtues that the English so liked to see in their own history.
THIS VALUES-FADE is at the core of Mr. Ferguson's story; he even refers to a "moral descent." Thus "The
War of the World" brings to mind "The Thirties" by Malcolm Muggeridge, a forgotten gem written in a spirit
almost of religious satire about what had happened to Old England and the civilization of Europe. What got
Muggeridge going was the contrast between pretension and reality. Here was a decade claiming to believe in
certain things, and each was false. The French Republic? Rat-trap faces with umbrellas and checkbooks. The
U.S.S.R.? Four lies in four initials. The gold standard? Dollars hidden under the bed. Capitalism? The slump.
German democracy? Weaselly politicians and an old field-marshal.
The League of Nations was a characteristic product of the era. It greeted the outbreak of World War II by
discussing how to standardize the level of railroad crossings. As the 20th century got under way, the older
European values were undermined, but it was still in their name -- progress most of all but a twisted sort of
"honor," too -- that Nazism and communism practiced their viciousness.
Whatever the woes of today, we are not now in the same set of difficulties and not as likely to harvest death
in the millions. In this case, History teaches respect not for the Past but for the Present. It sometimes
happens that way.
---
Mr. Stone, formerly professor of modern history at Oxford, teaches at Koc University in Istanbul, Turkey.

A Suburban Dad's African Kingdom --- Joseph Ngoupou, engineer and village chief, commutes from his Maryland home to rule his subjects --- On...
A Suburban Dad's African Kingdom --- Joseph Ngoupou, engineer and village chief, commutes from
his Maryland home to rule his subjects --- On the docket: a dispute over shoes
By Michael M. Phillips
3,154 words
23 September 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Koupa Ngagnou, Cameroon -- THE FACTS OF THE CASE weren't in question: one of the Chietemou
brothers trespassed on Dorine Ghariemgam's family land and poached a handful of plums. Ms. Ghariemgam
called him a thief and added that his mother had been one, too. Later, both Chietemou brothers, angry over
the insult to their late mother, jumped Ms. Ghariemgam in a farm field, beat her and broke her fertilizer
sprayer.
So the hearing last month wasn't about who did what to whom. Instead, it was about how to restore tranquility
to an isolated village where pretty much everyone is family. To do that, locals called in the one man they
knew whose authority and wisdom are unquestioned in Koupa Ngagnou -- a 56-year-old civil-aviation
consultant who lives nearly 6,000 miles away in suburban Silver Spring, Md.
While many Africans juggle ancient and modern ways, Joseph Ngoupou's schizophrenic life must rank among
the most extreme. In the U.S., he and his wife Aline, a budget officer at the World Bank, own a six-bedroom
home outside Washington, D.C., drive a shiny sport-utility vehicle and are taking up golf. But Mr. Ngoupou is
also hereditary chief of Koupa Ngagnou, a village in a Cameroonian kingdom that dates back to the 14th
century. Two or three times a year, he returns to his birthplace to resume his duties as father-figure and
benefactor, judge and jury for 3,500 villagers.
Like the best and brightest from other corners of the developing world, Africans who prosper in the global
economy often leave home to do so. But many maintain close ties to ancestral villages, where their relatives
still live by ancient traditions and survive mostly on what they can coax from the ground. While many parts of
Africa are wracked by strife, poverty and pestilence, many of its educated elite manage to transcend these
problems by living in two worlds. Yet even those who lead modern, urban lives -- in Nairobi, Paris or
Washington -- still frequently consider their villages to be home.
In the Baltimore-Washington area, the Census Bureau counts more than 150,000 African-born residents,
more than the number of European-born residents. Africans are among the fastest-growing group of
immigrants in the region. And in an era of cheap international communications and discount air tickets, it's
easier than ever for them to maintain ties -- and keep up their obligations -- to the lands they left.
Last month, Mr. Ngoupou and his wife traveled home to Koupa Ngagnou and stocked the village clinic with
medicine. They received dozens of impoverished petitioners asking for money to buy a bar of soap, a bit of
meat or some other necessity. Mr. Ngoupou supervised negotiations over the bride price for their son's
wedding, a ceremony complete with celebratory gunfire, raucous drumming and plenty of palm wine.
Most challenging, he held court to settle disputes, however small, that had accumulated during his absence.
"All of our strength is in unity," he counseled villagers who gathered to listen to him decide the case of the
purloined plums. "Division is weakness."
KOUPA NGAGNOU is spread among corn fields, mango groves and electric-green hills. It is five miles from
the nearest town, along a dirt lane often passable only on foot or by four-wheel-drive.
The 18-acre Ngoupou compound -- where members of the extended family live -- is a series of buildings,
some of cement, some of red-mud brick, clustered around the tombs of Mr. Ngoupou's parents, grandparents
and great-grandparents. There is no electrical service or piped water. To power lights, the Ngoupous use
propane and a small generator; they collect rainwater and filter it for drinking. Women cook over a wood fire
inside the smoky kitchen building, usually stews of chicken, fish or beef to liven up the cornmeal paste that is
the local staple.

--- Page 245 ---
Page 245 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Ngoupou is the sixth in his family to wear the feathered crown of Koupa Ngagnou's chief. His father, Jean
Poupie, was a clerk in the French colonial government's forestry service. Mr. Poupie took two wives, following
local custom, and fathered 16 children.
Mr. Ngoupou attended a missionary school and won a scholarship to study civil-aviation engineering in Niger.
His career led him to Cameroon's business capital, Douala, and to Senegal, where he worked for an
international air-traffic-control agency.
While in high school, he met his wife Aline, the converted Christian daughter of a wealthy Muslim coffee
farmer from a nearby village. The Ngoupous, who have six children, have spent much of their lives apart,
commuting between jobs. She studied financial management in France, landing a job that took her to World
Bank offices across Africa and to Washington. Mr. Ngoupou joined her there in 2004 and launched his
consulting firm.
In the U.S., Mr. and Mrs. Ngoupou, in a sense, apply their chieftaincy to suburban life. They are deacons at
their Pentecostal church, helping the many immigrants in the congregation adjust to life in America. Mr.
Ngoupou advises husbands to embrace what he sees as their biblical role as heads of their families; Mrs.
Ngoupou, 53, cooks them Cameroonian food.
"Washington people are so busy," she says. "People are running after money. They don't have time for an
emotional life. When you have an issue in Africa, you can stop by a cousin or auntie's house, and they'll listen
to you."
The couple leads weekly church support groups for African immigrant couples, on issues ranging from work
stress to women's rights to disrespectful children. Among other things, they offer tips on how to resolve spats.
It's an endeavor at which Mr. Ngoupou, a poet by avocation, is particularly experienced.
Even when he's in Maryland, Mr. Ngoupou closely tracks happenings back in Koupa Ngagnou. When a
conflict breaks out, his older cousin, Emmanuel Mbouomboud, or another trusted adviser calls with a report,
usually within hours.
Mr. Ngoupou lists the cases on his laptop computer to ensure they are dispatched in the same order that they
came in; to do otherwise would risk bruised feelings and more conflict. When possible, he asks Mr.
Mbouomboud to settle the disputes. But often they end up on the docket that awaits Mr. Ngoupou when he
returns to Cameroon.Their Maryland subdivision is quiet and close-knit; neighbors keep an eye on the
Ngoupous' home when the couple is away.
In Koupa Ngagnou, hearings take place in Mr. Ngoupo's living room, with its red cement floor, wood beams
and corrugated-metal roof. Dozens of villagers face Mr. Ngoupou, who occupies a large wicker chair under a
photo of himself as a younger man. Mrs. Ngoupou sits to his left, in a stuffed armchair, with various elders
flanking them. While there is a government court in a nearby town, villagers usually prefer that Mr. Ngoupou
handle their disputes.
The conflicts range widely, from whose goat is raiding whose garden to whose husband is sleeping with
whose wife. Last year, Mr. Ngoupou adjudicated a case in which a toddler destroyed his young cousin's shoe,
allegedly slicing it with a knife. For poor farmers, buying shoes can be a substantial burden, and the boys'
parents refused to speak to each other, each couple blaming the other's son.
"From a small problem you can get a big problem," says Mr. Ngoupou. At the hearing, he calmed tempers
and reminded everyone that boys will, after all, be boys. He ordered the parents to let them remain friends,
and he paid for a new pair of shoes.
The pressure is enormous for a judge who is related to virtually all of those he judges, and Mr. Ngoupou tries
to avoid condemning anyone. "Sometimes it is very, very difficult to solve," he says. "Everyone is expecting to
be right."
Late in his own reign, Mr. Ngoupou's father, Mr. Poupie, split the difference in a bitter land dispute between
two branches of a family. Both sides rejected the ruling and sulked, and Mr. Poupie sank into depression and
died a couple of years later, in 1989. Now the disputed parcel lies fallow because, Mr. Ngoupou says,
villagers believe the dispute hastened Mr. Poupie's death. "Everyone is afraid of bringing it up," he says.
After Mr. Poupie's death, representatives of the area's traditional ruler, King Seidou Njimoluh Njoya -- the
18th monarch in the kingdom of Foumban -- summoned the late chief's children to Koupa Ngagnou. There,
they would reveal the identity of his designated heir.
The Ngoupous brought a gift to give whomever Mr. Poupie had chosen. They say they were stunned when
the king's man read the secret will and announced that the next chief would be Mr. Ngoupou, who was then
39 years old and living hundreds of miles away in Niger.

--- Page 246 ---
Page 246 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"No!" Mrs. Ngoupou recalls shouting. "It's not him. It's a mistake."
It wasn't, however, a job Mr. Ngoupou could turn down -- even though he hadn't lived in Koupa Ngagnou
full-time since 1969. He moved home for a year and implemented changes to bring the chieftaincy more in
line with his own Evangelical Christian beliefs.
Villagers, he instructed, were no longer to approach him on hand and knee, the way they had his father. He
banned some traditions of ancestor worship, such as summoning spirits from the river and conducting animal
sacrifices. Many villagers were already Christians, and he told everyone to attend church, instructing them
that they were only to kneel before God.
MRS. NGOUPOU QUICKLY realized that being the chief's wife -- and, in the eyes of many villagers,
impossibly wealthy -- was almost as burdensome as being the chief. Each morning neighbors, friends and
relatives stop by the house asking for money. "The people here are poor," she says. "You have to feed them
and give them drinks. They don't just visit you and go."
Cameroon has oil reserves, rich farmland and an average annual income that, at $2,400, makes the country
well-to-do by West African standards. But almost half of Cameroonians live below the poverty line. While the
country has moved slowly towards democratic reforms, villagers expect and receive little from their
government. They expect and receive much from family.
DURING HER RECENT VISIT, Mrs. Ngoupou delivered hundreds of dollars in antibiotics, malaria drugs,
latex gloves and other supplies to the clinic, a small building furnished with beds made of bamboo stalks
lashed together. The clinic is just one of several village-improvement efforts the couple finances.
The Ngoupous don't get paid for their village roles. In fact, with airfare, phone bills and charitable works, they
figure they spend more than $15,000 a year on Koupa Ngagnou. "People think I have dollars falling out of my
boubou," says Mrs. Ngoupou, shaking the loose, violet-and-gold sleeves of her robe.
Equally challenging for the Ngoupous has been the struggle to ensure that their children get the best of both
their worlds: the sense of community that comes with life in the village, and the opportunities that come with
life in the West.
The couple generally has their children remain with relatives in Cameroon through high school to ensure they
know the culture and learn the language spoken in the village, Bamoun. The exception is their son Antoine,
22, who has lived much of his life in either Senegal or Maryland. He attended Oral Roberts University but
says he left after becoming fed up with the Oklahoma Christian school's rules on dress and behavior.
Antoine, who plans to finish his studies in Paris, visits Cameroon about once every two years, enough to stay
in touch but not enough to be fully fluent in village ways.
Accompanying his parents on their last trip, he made the mistake of giving kola nuts to the father of a pretty
girl named Barbara. In return, Barbara's father gave him peppercorns and told him to have his mother use
them to cook a meal. Antoine knew a gift of kola nuts constitutes an expression of romantic interest. But he
didn't appreciate that it is tantamount to an offer of marriage; eating the peppercorns would seal the deal.
"When I gave it to him, I wasn't thinking like that," he said as his mother looked at the peppercorns on her
coffee table and pondered her son's faux pas.
"We already have someone married into that family," Mrs. Ngoupou told him. "I'm not going into that family
again for another girl."
Antoine isn't fluent in the local language either. During a gathering last month, his father's cousin, Mr.
Mbouomboud, lamented that "some sons of the village cannot speak Bamoun."
Mr. Ngoupou then instructed Antoine, who speaks French and English, to address the crowd in Bamoun.
Antoine, wearing baggy Michael Jordan shorts and a Ralph Lauren T-shirt, wiped his brow and stumbled
through a few words. "It is a pleasure for me to see my friends and family," he said, before his father let him
off the hook.
In addition to dealing with village business on their last visit, the Ngoupous held the wedding of their oldest
son, Guy-Serges Ngoupou, 30, a marketing manager for a home-security firm in Paris.
In France, where he has lived for more than a decade, Guy-Serges feels he has to balance cultural
assimilation with cultural preservation. In the village he eats with his right hand; in France he eats with a fork.
If he visits an African family in France and eats with a fork, "they'll think I'm pretending to be white," he says.
If he wears a boubou robe while out with his white friends, they accuse him of playing the African.

--- Page 247 ---
Page 247 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
He first spotted his bride, 22-year-old Christelle-Danielle Njankoue, at a Cameroonian airport. He was on his
way back to Paris; she was returning to her studies in Canada. He later popped the question in a Paris
restaurant.
Wedding planning fell largely to the senior Ngoupous, who opted for civil and black-tie church events in the
capital city, and a traditional wedding in the bride's village. The village ceremony began when the extended
Ngoupou family arrived carrying on their heads cases of beer and Coke, jugs of palm wine and cooking oil,
sacks of corn, a bag of kola nuts, a blanket and other gifts for the bride's family. Musicians, some in feathered
headdresses, set the beat on drums, gourds and wooden xylophones.
Inside the house, Mr. Ngoupou and the other men of the family sat in plastic chairs opposite the men of the
bride's family. Guy-Serges nervously chewed his lip and double-checked to make sure he had turned off his
cellphone.
OVER THE NEXT HOUR, four marital negotiators went through a cheerful ritual, led by the bride's uncle,
Misaac Njoya. After examining the food and drink the Ngoupous brought, the bride's family approved the
union -- in principle. What remained was to settle on the bride price, a traditional custom. After debate, some
dismissive gestures and a mock machete fight between two musicians, the bride's uncle handed the groom a
piece of paper. On it was written in Bamoun: "The price of a woman is love, togetherness and patience."
It was a price both families could agree on. "It's done -- we bought her," Mr. Ngoupou beamed.
The overlapping ties of family and village reinforce Mr. Ngoupou's authority and complicate his work resolving
disputes, such as the plum case. Ms. Ghariemgam, the 32-year-old woman who was attacked, is a cousin of
the Chietemou brothers, who attacked her. She is also Mr. Ngoupou's half-sister; her mother was Chief
Poupie's junior wife.
Initially Ms. Ghariemgam went to the police to demand the brothers be arrested. When Mr. Ngoupou, then in
the U.S., caught wind of it, he dispatched Mr. Mbouomboud to the police station, where officers agreed to
leave the matter to the family. The grievance simmered for almost a year until Mr. Ngoupou took it up.
During the two-and-a-half-hour hearing, Ms. Ghariemgam talked loudly over others, angry they didn't seem to
appreciate she had been wronged. "Since you all consider me an outcast, take my name off the family list,"
she said.
Mr. Ngoupou listened calmly. Then he asked her to drink from a glass of water, a gesture understood to
demonstrate his authority. She complied, and he perched her on the arm of Mrs. Ngoupou's chair. "You have
to give a little for the sake of family peace," he told her.
Mrs. Ngoupou sent around a jug of palm wine and a tray of spicy corn cakes to break the tension.
Ms. Ghariemgam sat fuming, until she again burst out in invective. Mr. Ngoupou's younger brother leapt to his
feet, saying he might thrash her for lack of respect for the chief. It wasn't an idle threat; two years ago, the
same brother gave her 100 lashes with a stick over another display of disrespect, according to Mr. Ngoupou.
This time Mrs. Ngoupou intervened. "Even President Bush knows you cannot walk by yourself," she told Ms.
Ghariemgam, who stalked out of the room in tears and continued her tirade beside her grandparents' tomb.
"I'm a curse upon the family," she yelled. "Nobody understands me."
Mr. Ngoupou ordered other parties to the dispute -- 29-year-old Valentin Chietemou, Ms. Ghariemgam's
75-year-old mother, and another cousin -- to share a glass of water, symbolically ending the disagreement.
The Chietemous had already paid Ms. Ghariemgam $50 for having broken her fertilizer sprayer.
"We're all different," the chief said. "But we should work together toward the same goal -- peace in the
village."
For Mr. Chietemou, the chief's ruling was final. "As far as I'm concerned, I'm not mad at her anymore," he
said.
Mr. Ngoupou dealt with Ms. Ghariemgam separately. He knew she was hurt that he hadn't immediately taken
her side. But, as chief, he felt he had to be even-handed or risk losing his authority.
"When you're in the Western world, it's written down what you do and don't do to live in peace," he explained
later, noting wistfully that he found life far more relaxing in Maryland than in Cameroon. "But in the village it's
not written. Human beings are more complex than anything you could write down."

Masters of All Universes
WEEKEND JOURNAL
Masters of All Universes
By Jon Weinbach
3,426 words
22 September 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
W1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[They're players outside the office, too. Jon Weinbach finds the executive suite's top golfer, triathlete, tennis
player, even stunt pilot.]
FOR MOST PEOPLE, work-life balance means packing lunch for the kids, doing solid work at the office and
sneaking an occasional trip to the gym.
For Zan Guerry, it means running a $280 million consumer-products company and still finding time to
maintain his status as one of the country's top senior tennis players. The 57-year-old CEO of Chattem Inc.
just won a doubles title at the U.S. Grass Court championships outside Philadelphia, and has been the United
States Tennis Association's No. 1 singles player in three different age groups. "I just happen to love the
game, and I like to win," Mr. Guerry says.
CEOs are paid millions of dollars a year, travel on plush private jets and are catered to by attentive staffs.
Here's another reason to hate them: Some will also trounce you at your favorite sport. Senior executives from
companies such as Bear Stearns, Micron Technology and Toyota are challenging themselves in competitive
pursuits from golf and tennis to stunt flying -- and in some cases beating full-time professionals.
Anthony Hsieh, president of online mortgage giant LendingTree.com, with about $370 million in revenue last
year, recently pocketed about $200,000 in prize money after winning a major competitive sport-fishing event.
Ken Block, the co-founder and chief brand officer of DC Shoes, a division of publicly traded Quiksilver Inc.,
took the wheel of a rally racing car at this summer's X Games and went home with a bronze medal. There's
even a cutthroat bowler: Dallas real-estate mogul John Amend employs a full-time coach, averages 220 and
brings in top pros to play in a weekly league with guest bowlers that have included Texas Rangers owner
Tom Hicks.
To find the executive suites' top competitors, we canvassed sports organizations and companies, focusing on
"C-Level" execs -- such as chief executive officer and chief operating officer -- who've been recognized for
excellence in their favorite pastimes. That means some talented vice presidents were ruled out, including
Nissan North America marketing vice president Jan Thompson, a 10-handicap golfer. Also omitted were
those who excel in activities where excellence is closely tied to spending, such as collecting art or wine, or
whose pursuits don't lead to scores, proficiency licenses or rankings. (That meant passing on Chris Clarke,
president of executive-search firm Boyden World Corp., who's also a world-class Scottish folk dancer.)
There have always been executives whose competitive streak extends beyond the boardroom, of course,
including yacht captains Ted Turner and Larry Ellison, and balloonist Richard Branson. But the influx of
top-flight executives into competitions mirrors a heightened national focus on fitness, and it also reflects the
growth in competitions for runners, swimmers, even poker players. The number of triathlons, a preferred
activity of "chief endurance officers" like Disney's CEO Robert Iger, has exploded: This year, USA Triathlon,
the sport's governing body, sanctioned more than 1,900 races, up from about 400 in 1999.
There's even a growing business in catering to gung-ho execs. CEO Challenges, a Boulder, Colo., firm that
organizes sports events for C-level managers, got its start five years ago hosting an Ironman race in Lake
Placid, N.Y., that attracted 15 participants. Now, its five-event triathlon schedule draws about 40 corporate
athletes. Next up, the company will stage its first nationally televised CEO Golf Challenge, and plans to host a
CEO fishing tournament next January in Belize.
Management experts say many executives are looking for an outlet and a buffer from a BlackBerry-driven,
24/7 work life. At the same time, these are hard-charging personalities: They're impelled to reach the top of
their company, and often can't stop once they get there. "They always need another target, another challenge
that puts their off-the-chart energy to use," says Sydney Finkelstein, a professor of strategy and leadership at
Dartmouth's Tuck School of Business.

--- Page 250 ---
Page 250 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But executives who play too hard or too publicly can find themselves in hot water. In 2001, for example,
director Walter Hewlett raised the ire of Hewlett-Packard board members when he skipped a vote on its
purchase of Compaq to play cello in a concert. That absence came after he missed a different meeting to
bike in a 129-mile California event called the "Death Ride." An executive extracurricular activity can become a
particularly sensitive issue at companies that are underperforming or weathering controversy.
"They don't hire you to be a CEO for your extracurricular skills," says Charles Elson, director of the Weinberg
Center for Corporate Governance at the University of Delaware. "For the extraordinary amount of money
they're making, it's reasonable to expect executives to focus on the job all the time."
A few executives with well-known side interests declined to be interviewed. According to the Web site for the
U.S. Golf Association's computerized handicap system, Nationwide Bank CEO Jerry Jurgensen has played
20 rounds since late July, and is now better than a so-called scratch golfer, with a "plus" handicap of 0.6
strokes. And Lucent chief Patricia Russo has a 13 handicap. A spokeswoman for Ms. Russo said she hasn't
been playing much recently because of the company's pending merger with Alcatel.
Others remain committed to their hobbies no matter what. Steve Appleton, the chairman and chief executive
of Micron Technology, nearly died when his plane crashed when he was performing an aerial exhibition for a
corporate video in 2004. His injuries included a broken back, a collapsed lung, torn cartilage throughout his
sternum and a two-inch laceration on his forehead. "I'm obviously an aggressive person," says Mr. Appleton,
46, who has Federal Aviation Administration clearance to execute aviation tricks. "It's kind of a cliche, but I'd
rather die living than die dying."
Here's a look at senior executives who are recognized as top performers in their extracurricular activities:
Rally Racing
Ken Block, 38, co-founder and chief brand officer, DC Shoes, Vista, Calif.
There's no shortage of business and entertainment titans who have been smitten with fast cars. David
Letterman has a stake in an Indy Racing League team, and veteran media executive Leo Hindery was part of
a three-driver squad that won its division at last year's 24 Hours of Le Mans race. But few have competed as
successfully against professional drivers as Ken Block.
At last month's X Games in Los Angeles, Mr. Block finished third -- just a few minutes behind winner Travis
Pastrana -- in rally racing, an event that requires drivers to race against the clock on dirt courses, often in
remote areas. (It's big in Europe and South America.) Mr. Block, who started driving rally cars only two years
ago, won the 2005 Rookie of the Year in Rally America, the top U.S. series, and he's currently third in points
this season out of more than 60 drivers.
He also works full time as chief brand officer at DC Shoes, the 150-employee company he co-founded in
1994. Quiksilver bought the company in 2004, and earlier this year Mr. Block gave up the role of company
president, but he continues to review shoe designs and direct DC's marketing. (Mr. Block helped create many
of the company's earliest skateboarding shoes.) Quiksilver, based in Huntington Beach, Calif., announced
earlier this month that earnings per share for the third quarter decreased 80% compared with 2005, and
lowered its fourth-quarter earnings targets, largely because of slower sales at its Rossignol ski-equipment
brand. Mr. Block says his racing schedule -- about 10 weekends a year -- doesn't interfere with his corporate
responsibilities. "I can work well with a laptop," he says.
Mr. Block has had at least two serious crashes during races, including a spill last year when his car hit a tree
while turning a corner, spun 180 degrees and slammed into another tree. "We could slow down," Mr. Block
says. "But then we couldn't be winning races."
Aerobatic Flying
Steve Appleton, 46, chairman, CEO and president, Micron Technology Inc., Boise, Idaho
At most corporate retreats, team-building exercises tend toward karaoke nights and trust walks. At Micron
outings, Mr. Appleton has led the memory-chip manufacturer's managers in full-contact ice hockey and
off-road-vehicle racing on the coast of Mexico. During one event, a dirt-bike ride in Arizona, Micron's chief
operating officer skidded during a turn and broke several ribs -- and had a second biking accident at last
year's corporate retreat near Moab, Utah. "We're a hard-charging group," says the oft-injured operating chief,
Mark Durcan. "It all starts at the top."
So far Mr. Durcan has declined to take part in his CEO's after-hours obsession: Mr. Appleton is one of the
country's most accomplished aerobatic pilots. Only experienced fliers are allowed to take beginner aerobatics
courses, and it takes several competency cards from the FAA to earn the right to do the kind of tricks that Mr.
Appleton performs -- loops, rolls and inverted stalls at altitudes below 100 feet. Mr. Appleton's 2004 crash

--- Page 251 ---
Page 251 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
didn't end his aerobatic career, and he still flies at five to six air shows a year, often in a red Hawker Hunter
fighter jet that he bought from the Singapore Air Force in the '90s.
Mr. Appleton jokes that one of the few things more hazardous than flying is the memory-chip business. He
has diversified Micron Technology's product base, moving to become a key supplier of technology for camera
phones and mobile media devices. The company's stock is up 33% since January. Douglas Freedman,
managing director of American Technology Research in San Francisco, sees an investor benefit in Mr.
Appleton's hobby. "He's more available than most CEOs because he can just fly himself to a meeting," Mr.
Freedman says. "His travel agent is the FAA."
Triathlon
Jamie Maguire, 46, president and CEO, Philadelphia Insurance Companies, Philadelphia
In a crowded field of executive triathletes, we gave the nod to Mr. Maguire, who'll likely complete six triathlons
this year, including the Ironman World Championship in Hawaii next month. He qualified for that event by
finishing fourth in his age group -- out of more than 219 racers -- at last December's Ironman Florida race, in
nine hours and 54 minutes. That's faster than times posted by other notable triathletes, including Jarden CEO
Martin Franklin, Denver Broncos owner Pat Bowlen and Citadel Group chief David Varwig, who once hired
one of Lance Armstrong's advisers to build him a bike.
The insurance-company boss has used triathlons to motivate workers. The company pays entry fees and
hotel costs for employees who take part in Ironman races, covers half the cost of gym memberships and
allows lunchtime workouts. Three times a week, Mr. Maguire leads a group of managers in early-morning
bike rides and midday pool sessions. So far, he says, about 100 employees have entered distance runs, and
about 12 others will compete in triathlons this year. "If you can challenge yourself physically, that has overflow
into the business world."
While training for his first Ironman race in 2001, the insurance-company boss suffered a broken neck, jaw,
kneecap and left hand after a driver hit him during a morning cycling workout. With his jaw wired shut, he
could eat only liquids for eight weeks. Though he says biking is still his strong suit, he returned to cycling with
trepidation. "You just have to keep your cool because it's real easy to panic," he says.
Bridge
Jimmy Cayne, 72, chairman and CEO, Bear Stearns Cos., New York
Mr. Cayne is the oldest member of our group, and likely the only one who hones his game while sitting at a
computer, chomping on a cigar. But two or three times a week, the most powerful man at Bear Stearns can
be found playing online bridge against competitors from around the world.
In many ways, he owes his Wall Street career to the game. For several years in his early 30s, Mr. Cayne was
a professional bridge player, hustling games all over Manhattan. He eventually scored an invitation to play at
the home of Laurence Tisch, the late former chairman of Loews Corp. When he got wind of an opportunity at
Bear Stearns, he interviewed with Alan "Ace" Greenberg, then a rising company executive. The two men
discovered their shared passion for bridge, launching a personal and professional relationship that continues
to this day.
Mr. Cayne isn't as high-ranked as he was during the '70s and '80s, but he's won 11 national bridge
tournaments in his career and has played in several "Bermuda Bowl" competitions, the game's most
prestigious team event. At the Spingold tournament this past July, one of the three big events on the U.S.
circuit, his team finished second.
Over the years, he's ruffled some feathers by paying top players to compete on his team, though there's no
prize money awarded at bridge tournaments. (Several other players "sponsor" teams as well; Mr. Cayne
wouldn't comment on payments for players.)
Mr. Cayne wishes more young people played bridge -- which he calls "mind-boggling and beautiful and
frustrating" -- and laments the rise of poker, which he dubs a "semi-mindless game."
Bowling
John Amend, 57, president, Amend Group, Dallas
Mr. Amend tends to do things in a Texas-size way. His real-estate company was one of Dallas's
fastest-growing companies, and then downsized quickly after its biggest client, WorldCom, went bust. His
home is a 10-acre replica of George Washington's estate -- it, too, is called Mount Vernon -- that once
belonged to legendary oilman H.L. Hunt. So when he rediscovered bowling a few years ago, he built a $1.5

--- Page 252 ---
Page 252 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
million, 5,000-square-foot home bowling alley with four lanes, a brick pizza oven and an 82-inch
high-definition TV. He also brought in a full-time coach and began inviting the sport's top players for
exhibitions.
His game soon improved dramatically. His average is 220 a game -- a perfect score is 300 -- and last year,
he bowled a 258 to tie his guest Chris Barnes, who's won several titles on the Professional Bowlers
Association tour. Mr. Amend says bowling has supplanted everything else in his exercise regimen, and that
the sport is "almost aerobic" when he practices alone. Inviting others provides a different benefit. "Bowling
enables you to relate to people in a way you can't at basketball games, football games, or even on the golf
course," he says.
His Wednesday-night CEO league is now in its fifth season, and several Dallas-area business leaders have
played, including Rangers owner Mr. Hicks and Marty Weiland, chairman of Northern Trust Bank of Texas.
"It's gotten to the point where people aren't just competitive, but they're actually pretty good," says Mr.
Weiland, whose average is around 175. "It's not just about standing there and eating nachos."
Tennis
Zan Guerry, 57, chairman and CEO, Chattem Inc., Chattanooga, Tenn.
Mr. Guerry starred on the junior circuit and at Rice University. He turned pro briefly in the '70s, and once beat
John McEnroe to qualify for the U.S. Open. He eventually went to Wharton Business School and settled at
Chattem, where his father -- also a tennis player -- had served as president since 1958. In 1990, he took over
the company, maker of Selsun Blue shampoo, Gold Bond powders and lotions, and the Icy Hot line of
muscle-pain relievers. "I obviously test all the new stuff out," he says.
One reason he can devote time to tennis: The publicly traded company, with a market capitalization of $650
million, manufactures about 60% of its products in Chattanooga, so Mr. Guerry doesn't have the travel
demands of other CEOs. In 2005, sales increased 8% to $279 million.
While balancing work and a competitive schedule would seem to leave little time for anything else, Mr.
Guerry's wife says her husband's commitment to tennis helps foster a balance between the office and home.
"He says he's not attached to outcome, but he's in a better mood when he wins," says Julie Guerry, who was
a competitive swimmer in high school. She says tennis also gives him a sense of individual control that can't
be replicated in a team-oriented corporate setting. Her only rule: "I don't ever play with him," she says.
---
For CEOs, Unique Skill Sets
Here are four other executive achievers:
GABRIEL SCHWARTZMAN, 29, CEO, Barr Systems, Gainesville, Fla.
Achievement: Became chess grandmaster as a teen.
At 17, Mr. Schwartzman, a native of Romania, was the world's youngest chess grandmaster. But he gave up
the idea of a career in pro chess after winning the U.S. Open of chess as an undergraduate at the University
of Florida; the prize money was only about $5,000.
After college, he began working at Barr, which makes software and hardware for corporate printing networks
and data systems; he was promoted a few months ago to CEO. He recently tried to use chess as a
morale-builder, taking on 10 employees at once during lunch. "It was supposed to be a kind of teamwork
exercise," he says. "But I whupped everyone pretty easily."
JIM PRESS, 59, president, Toyota Motor North America, New York
Achievement: In May, swam a 50-meter freestyle race in under 30 seconds.
Mr. Press says his hobby is running Toyota -- he's the first American to oversee all of its North American
operations at a time when the company continues to gain market share against U.S. auto makers. But he
says his real job is swimming. (He isn't straying far from the corporate playbook: Longtime Toyota CEO and
Chairman Eiji Toyota was an enthusiastic swimmer.) Mr. Press fits in two pool sessions a day, 90 minutes to
two hours each, and has been competing in Masters events since the early '90s.
His assistants know not to arrange his schedule without leaving time for the pool. "I'm grumpy when I don't
swim. The people around me probably like it when I can get in the water." Though he hasn't won big races,
he says: "If I had more time, I could get on the podium. I'd like to be No. 1."

--- Page 253 ---
Page 253 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
CURT CULVER, 54, CEO of MGIC Investment Corp., Milwaukee
Achievement: Once beat Arnold Palmer in nine holes.
Among executive golfers, Mr. Culver rises to the top: He has won 25 championships at nine clubs and
maintains a microscopic handicap of just over two strokes. Golf "makes me a better executive," says Mr.
Culver, whose company manages nearly $170 billion in mortgage insurance.
"If I couldn't play golf, I wouldn't do this job -- it keeps you real because it's such a humbling game." The
real-estate market has been humbling, too. MGIC's revenue fell last quarter as many owners refinanced loans
while interest rates were low, and didn't renew their mortgage insurance policies. MGIC's share price is
down about 16% since January, but Mr. Culver says he thinks revenue is on the upswing.
ANTHONY HSIEH, 41, president, LendingTree.com
Achievement: His boats have won over $1 million in sport-fishing tournaments.
Mr. Hsieh has been hooking marlin for two decades and owns four boats, each named Bad Company. In
addition to weekend trips near his Newport Beach, Calif., home, he spends vacation time each July and
October at tournaments in Hawaii and Mexico. His teams compete in about eight events a year; at the World
Championship Billfish Release Tournament this May in Cabo, his boat caught 30 marlin in three days to win
first prize. "The ocean is contagious," he says.
Mr. Hsieh figures annual maintenance on his boats is "way beyond" the $1 million his teams have won. His
company, a unit of Barry Diller's IAC/InterActiveCorp., has also been affected by the slowing real-estate
market. In the second quarter, Lendingtree.com's profit dropped nearly 30% compared with the same period
in 2005.

Some Investors Lose Their Zest For Commodities --- Natural-Gas Debacle at Amaranth May Signal Broad Price Declines; 'Most Were Just...
Commodities Report
Some Investors Lose Their Zest For Commodities --- Natural-Gas Debacle at Amaranth May Signal
Broad Price Declines; 'Most Were Just Speculators'
By Gregory Zuckerman and Henny Sender
999 words
21 September 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
ON THE HEELS of natural-gas losses at Amaranth Advisors and other hedge funds and a tumble in
numerous commodities, some investors are selling off such holdings in a shift that could send prices lower if it
turns into a rush for the exits.
After long shying away from oil, natural gas, metals and other raw materials, investors of all stripes -- hedge
funds, pension plans, endowments and individual investors -- have become enamored with commodity
investing. These investors, including short-term speculators, have become key in various markets, sometimes
driving prices more than industrial customers who buy the materials to make things or sell services.
How these Johnny-come-lately investors react now "will have an effect on users, commercial producers, as
well as investors," says Howard Simons, a strategist at Chicago-based Bianco Research. "The flood of
money that's come in is out of scale to anything in the past, and most were just speculators."
There are 68 commodity-oriented hedge funds, up from 29 just three years ago, according to Hedge Fund
Research Inc. Those figures don't include the growing number of managed-futures funds and so-called
multistrategy hedge funds, like Amaranth, that also deal in commodities.
As for pension funds, "until 2003, there wasn't a whole heck of a lot of interest in commodities," says Neil
Rue, principal at Pension Consulting Alliance Inc. in Los Angeles. "But commodities are becoming a major
asset class and investments in the area have multiplied since 2003. It wasn't 10% or 5% a year, but much
more than that." Mr. Rue cautioned pension-plan clients to be wary of commodities.
Much as they did with tech-oriented investments shortly before they tanked in 2000, individual investors also
have rushed into commodities, via stocks of commodity-related companies and mutual funds that specialize
in such investments. There are 48 mutual funds that invest in commodities and related shares managing $56
billion, up from 34 funds with less than $10 billion three years ago, according to fund tracker Morningstar Inc.
The Commodity Real Return fund of Allianz AG's Pacific Investment Management Co. has grown to more
than $12.2 billion, from $8 billion about a year ago.
The 13th-largest holder of gold in the world isn't a central bank but an exchange-traded fund, a type of
security that trades like a stock and tracks the price of an underlying investment class. StreetracksGold Trust,
the largest gold ETF, has assets of $7.5 billion, up from $2.7 billion a year ago, mostly from new investments.
For evidence of these investors' influence, consider the Goldman Sachs commodity index, one of the most
popular vehicles for betting on raw materials. In July, Goldman Sachs tweaked the index's content to by
cutting its exposure to gasoline. Investors tracking the index had to adjust their portfolios accordingly -- which
sent gasoline futures prices tumbling.
Some investors entered these markets because they saw a long-term undersupply of a range of
commodities, including oil, as economic growth in China and India increased demand. But others were less
interested in such fundamentals and shifted in simply because commodities prices had gone up in recent
years, hoping to catch the next wave. Low interest rates made it possible for hedge funds to borrow at
attractive rates and invest in almost anything.
Lead illustrates the impact: It's basically industrial waste, the unloved byproduct of processing copper and
gold. But prices for lead -- mostly used in batteries, primarily for vehicles -- have more than doubled in the
past five years, even though stockpiles are high.
Now there are signs that some of that "hot" money is exiting the market.

--- Page 257 ---
Page 257 of 259 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"Large speculators began to liquidate gold and silver," wrote Mary Ann Bartels, a Merrill Lynch analyst, in a
report this week. "But there are no signs of panic that accompany a bottom."
The gold ETF has seen little new money in the past month. "The luster is off this sector, people are suddenly
realizing that gold-fund returns will not be as good as they've been," says Jeff Tjornehoj, an analyst at mutual
fund tracker Lipper.
Merrill Lynch's research suggests that hedge funds that speculate in oil have been doing some selling lately,
but many actually added to their natural-gas positions while keeping their heating-oil positions unchanged. Oil
futures dipped below $60 a barrel during yesterday's trading session and closed at $60.46, down $1.20.
The Pimco commodity fund is seeing little new investments lately, in part because it's down almost 7% this
year, though it also hasn't seen much in the way of withdrawals, says portfolio manager John Brynjolfsson,
calling fund flows "relatively steady and uneventful."
A fall in commodities prices might not be all bad news. Though a rush for the exits could cause pain for
commodities investors, an additional decline in the cost of energy and industrial metals could give a shot in
the arm to the global economy. And money moved out of commodities could shift into the stock market,
sending shares higher.
The end of this month could be key. Many commodity-oriented hedge funds let investors withdraw money
monthly or quarterly, so if losses persist, there may be big withdrawals. That could cause more carnage in the
hedge-fund world.
Amaranth's woes were caused by bad bets that natural-gas prices would rebound. Yesterday, natural-gas
futures continued reacting to a large selloff of positions by Amaranth. October natural-gas futures on the New
York Mercantile Exchange dropped 7.5 cents to settle at $4.93 per million British thermal units. November
gas futures settled 18 cents down at $6.02/MMBtu, December futures dropped 22.1 cents to $7.66/MMBtu
and January fell 23.6 cents to $8.20/MMBtu.

having held separate discussions with Yahoo, Microsoft and Viacom over the past year. Behind the pursuit of
